        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.2c21557"><meta property="og:title" content="Concentrated Nonaqueous Polyelectrolyte Solutions: High Na-Ion Transference Number and Surface-Tethered Polyanion Layer for Sodium-Metal Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/acsami.2c21557.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Na metal is a promising anode material for the preparation of next-generation high-energy-density sodium-ion batteries; however, the high reactivity of Na metal severely limits the choice of electrolyte. In addition, rapid charge–discharge battery systems require electrolytes with high Na-ion transport properties. Herein, we demonstrate a stable and high-rate sodium-metal battery enabled by a nonaqueous polyelectrolyte solution composed of a weakly coordinating polyanion-type Na salt, poly[(4-styrenesulfonyl)-(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide] (poly(NaSTFSI)) copolymerized with butyl acrylate, in a propylene carbonate solution. It was found that this concentrated polyelectrolyte solution exhibited a remarkably high Na-ion transference number (tNaPP = 0.9) and a high ionic conductivity (σ = 1.1 mS cm–1) at 60 °C. Furthermore, the surface of the Na electrode was modified with polyanion chains anchored via the partial decomposition of the electrolyte. The surface-tethered polyanion layer effectively suppressed the subsequent decomposition of the electrolyte, thereby enabling stable Na deposition/dissolution cycling. Finally, an assembled sodium-metal battery with a Na0.44MnO2 cathode demonstrated an outstanding charge/discharge reversibility (Coulombic efficiency >99.8%) over 200 cycles while also exhibiting a high discharge rate (i.e., 45% capacity retention at 10 mA cm–2)."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/acsami.2c21557.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90da89e9fdc9b325-MAN"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da89e9fdc9b325-MAN">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '3caeef92-6cab-4490-8749-2e1d1b75096e'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

type="text/x-mathjax-config;executed=true">MathJax.Hub.Config({
            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

        <script async="" type="text/javascript" src="https://www.googletagservices.com/tag/js/gpt.js" rel="preconnect" nonce="90da89e9fdc9b325-MAN"></script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da89e9fdc9b325-MAN">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

<script nonce="90da89e9fdc9b325-MAN">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Concentrated Nonaqueous Polyelectrolyte Solutions: High Na-Ion Transference Number and Surface-Tethered Polyanion Layer for Sodium-Metal Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c21557", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Concentrated Nonaqueous Polyelectrolyte Solutions: High Na-Ion Transference Number and Surface-Tethered Polyanion Layer for Sodium-Metal Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c21557", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Concentrated Nonaqueous Polyelectrolyte Solutions: High Na-Ion Transference Number and Surface-Tethered Polyanion Layer for Sodium-Metal Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c21557", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Concentrated Nonaqueous Polyelectrolyte Solutions: High Na-Ion Transference Number and Surface-Tethered Polyanion Layer for Sodium-Metal Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c21557", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Concentrated Nonaqueous Polyelectrolyte Solutions: High Na-Ion Transference Number and Surface-Tethered Polyanion Layer for Sodium-Metal Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c21557", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Concentrated Nonaqueous Polyelectrolyte Solutions: High Na-Ion Transference Number and Surface-Tethered Polyanion Layer for Sodium-Metal Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c21557", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Concentrated Nonaqueous Polyelectrolyte Solutions: High Na-Ion Transference Number and Surface-Tethered Polyanion Layer for Sodium-Metal Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c21557", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Concentrated Nonaqueous Polyelectrolyte Solutions: High Na-Ion Transference Number and Surface-Tethered Polyanion Layer for Sodium-Metal Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c21557", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Concentrated Nonaqueous Polyelectrolyte Solutions: High Na-Ion Transference Number and Surface-Tethered Polyanion Layer for Sodium-Metal Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c21557", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D11741%26pageCount%3D15%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DShinji%2BKondou%252C%2BYusuke%2BSakashita%252C%2BAsuka%2BMorinaga%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D15%26issueNum%3D9%26contentID%3Dacsami.2c21557%26title%3DConcentrated%2BNonaqueous%2BPolyelectrolyte%2BSolutions%253A%2BHigh%2BNa-Ion%2BTransference%2BNumber%2Band%2BSurface-Tethered%2BPolyanion%2BLayer%2Bfor%2BSodium-Metal%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D15%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D11755%26publicationDate%3DMarch%2B2023" Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/medium/am2c21557_0013.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/large/am2c21557_0013.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c21557&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Na metal is a promising anode material for the preparation of next-generation high-energy-density sodium-ion batteries; however, the high reactivity of Na metal severely limits the choice of electrolyte. In addition, rapid charge–discharge battery systems require electrolytes with high Na-ion transport properties. Herein, we demonstrate a stable and high-rate sodium-metal battery enabled by a nonaqueous polyelectrolyte solution composed of a weakly coordinating polyanion-type Na salt, poly[(4-styrenesulfonyl)-(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide] (poly(NaSTFSI)) copolymerized with butyl acrylate, in a propylene carbonate solution. It was found that this concentrated polyelectrolyte solution exhibited a remarkably high Na-ion transference number (<i>t</i><sub class="stack">Na</sub><sup class="stack">PP</sup> = 0.9) and a high ionic conductivity (σ = 1.1 mS cm<sup>–1</sup>) at 60 °C. Furthermore, the surface of the Na electrode was modified with polyanion chains anchored via the partial decomposition of the electrolyte. The surface-tethered polyanion layer effectively suppressed the subsequent decomposition of the electrolyte, thereby enabling stable Na deposition/dissolution cycling. Finally, an assembled sodium-metal battery with a Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> cathode demonstrated an outstanding charge/discharge reversibility (Coulombic efficiency &gt;99.8%) over 200 cycles while also exhibiting a high discharge rate (i.e., 45% capacity retention at 10 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>).</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D11741%26pageCount%3D15%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DShinji%2BKondou%252C%2BYusuke%2BSakashita%252C%2BAsuka%2BMorinaga%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D15%26issueNum%3D9%26contentID%3Dacsami.2c21557%26title%3DConcentrated%2BNonaqueous%2BPolyelectrolyte%2BSolutions%253A%2BHigh%2BNa-Ion%2BTransference%2BNumber%2Band%2BSurface-Tethered%2BPolyanion%2BLayer%2Bfor%2BSodium-Metal%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D15%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D11755%26publicationDate%3DMarch%2B2023" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2023 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292120&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrolytes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrolytes" class="article__tags__link">Electrolytes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292200&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Layers" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Layers" class="article__tags__link">Layers</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291417&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Polyelectrolytes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Polyelectrolytes" class="article__tags__link">Polyelectrolytes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292475&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Polymers" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Polymers" class="article__tags__link">Polymers</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Ion+Transport&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_ion%20transport">ion transport</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-metal+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-metal%20battery">sodium-metal battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Polyanion&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_polyanion">polyanion</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Polyelectrolyte&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_polyelectrolyte">polyelectrolyte</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Transference+Number&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_transference%20number">transference number</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are promising alternatives to lithium-ion batteries owing to the natural abundance of Na resources available for large-scale energy storage systems. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> In particular, the use of Na metal as an anode material (1166 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and −2.71 V vs the standard hydrogen electrode, SHE) is expected to achieve high energy densities in sodium-metal batteries (SMBs). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3 ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 3 to 5">(3−5)</a> Considering the analogy between Li and Na ions, a common electrolyte formulation has been investigated using ∼1 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> of the Na salt dissolved in a carbonate-based solvent such as propylene carbonate (PC) or ethylene carbonate (EC). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a> However, carbonate-based electrolytes are incompatible with low-voltage anode materials such as hard carbon <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> and Na metal <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> owing to the formation of an unstable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI). In contrast, recent studies have found that a 1.0 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> solution of NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diglyme exhibits good compatibility with hard carbon <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> and Na metal <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> and may therefore be suitable for use as an electrolyte material; however, the relatively low oxidative stability of ether-based electrolytes presents challenges in terms of the generation of high-energy-density sodium batteries with high-voltage cathode materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> Further developments in terms of the electrolyte design are therefore required to produce a breakthrough in the area of SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p">In this context, the design of advanced electrolytes typically involves solutions with a high Na-ion transference number (<i>t</i><sub>Na</sub>), that is, the ratio of the current carried by Na ions to the total current, which is an important parameter in batteries along with the ionic conductivity. Ab initio molecular dynamics (MD) simulations have estimated that an ether-based electrolyte consisting of 1 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diglyme exhibits a <i>t</i><sub>Na</sub> of 0.33, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> indicating that the main component of the current is derived from the motion of unreactive anions. In such cases, the Na salt forms a large concentration gradient in the electrolyte upon rapid charge–discharge, which significantly reduces the power density and lifetime of the battery. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> Solid polymer electrolytes are a promising alternative to liquid electrolytes owing to their flexibility, high thermal stability, and high electrochemical stability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13 ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 13 and 14">(13,14)</a> Although traditional poly(ethylene oxide) (PEO)-based electrolytes have been extensively investigated, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> the strong coordination of the PEO chains to the Na ions leads to a low <i>t</i><sub>Na</sub> of ∼0.3. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> Numerous polymer designs have therefore been recently proposed to increase the Li- or Na-ion transference number (&gt;0.5) through regulation of the interactions between the metal ions and the polar groups of the polymer backbones (e.g., polycarbonates, polyesters, perfluoropolyethers, and polymerized ionic liquids). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 17 to 22">(17−22)</a> For example, a simple strategy to maximize <i>t</i><sub>Na</sub> was previously developed, wherein this system employs a single Na-conducting ionomer (<i>t</i><sub>Na</sub> ≈ 1) composed of anions that are covalently tethered to the immobile polymer backbone. In previous studies, polyanion-type Na salts have been used in the form of solid- or cross-linked gel-state electrolytes; <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref24 ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 23 to 25">(23−25)</a> however, such electrolytes suffer from low ionic conductivities (i.e., 10<sup>–8</sup> to 10<sup>–7</sup> S cm<sup>–1</sup> at 30 °C) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> owing to their low degree of ion dissociation as a result of counterion condensation. In contrast, nonaqueous polyelectrolyte solutions, in which polyanion-type Li salts are dissolved in nonaqueous solvents to enhance ionic dissociation, have recently been reported with conductivities ranging from 10<sup>–4</sup> to 10<sup>–3</sup> S cm<sup>–1</sup> at 25–30 °C, and these systems have also demonstrated a lower degree of anion-derived conductivity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 26 to 29">(26−29)</a> Importantly, batteries incorporating polyelectrolyte solutions can be manufactured by conventional processes owing to the liquid state of the polyelectrolyte, thereby removing the need to redesign the battery manufacturing process. However, despite the advantages of polyelectrolyte solutions, studies concerning the ion transport and electrochemical properties of nonaqueous Na-based polyelectrolyte solutions are rare, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> and their application in SIBs remains largely unexplored.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Accordingly, we herein report our investigation of the linear-chain weakly coordinating polyanion-type Na salt sodium poly[(4-styrenesulfonyl)-(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide] (poly(NaSTFSI)), which is employed as a polyelectrolyte in the PC solution. The ion transport properties and Na metal compatibility of this system are evaluated, and its Na-ion transport properties are explained in terms of the structural changes in the polyanions arising from the entanglement of the polyanion chains and counterion condensation. Furthermore, the electrochemical compatibility of this polyelectrolyte solution with Na metal is evaluated through the use of complementary surface analytical techniques. Finally, the cycle performances of the Na/Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and Na/hard carbon cells and the discharge rate performance of the Na/Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> cell are investigated.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Materials</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Sodium (4-styrenesulfonyl) (trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide (NaSTFSI) was kindly supplied by Tosoh Finechem Co. (Japan). Sodium bis(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide (NaTFSI) was prepared by neutralization of HTFSI and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>. Butyl acrylate (BA) was purchased from Tokyo Chemical Industry Co. (Japan), and the polymerization inhibitor was removed by column chromatography using alumina powder prior to use. 2,2′-Azobis(isobutyronitrile) (AIBN) was purchased from Wako Chemical Industry (Japan) and purified by repeated recrystallization from methanol. Dimethyl sulfoxide (DMSO) and PC were purchased from Kishida Chemical Co. (Japan). Pentaethylene glycol monomethyl ether (G5) was kindly supplied by Nippon Nyukazai.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Synthesis of the Poly(NaSTFSI-<i>r</i>-BA) Copolymer</h3><div class="NLM_p last">NaSTFSI (14.8 mmol) and BA (14.8 mmol) were dissolved in DMSO (40 mL), and the solution was added to an Ar-filled Schlenk flask. Any O<sub>2</sub> gas dissolved in the solution was then purged by bubbling Ar gas through the solution for 1 h. Subsequently, AIBN (0.03 mmol) was added, and the radical polymerization reaction was allowed to proceed at 60 °C for 24 h. After the removal of DMSO by vacuum distillation, the obtained random copolymer, poly(NaSTFSI-<i>r</i>-BA), was purified by repetitive reprecipitation using methanol (a good solvent) and diethyl ether (a poor solvent), and the obtained precipitate was dried under vacuum at 80 °C overnight to give the purified poly(NaSTFSI-<i>r</i>-BA) as a yellow powder in a yield of 72%.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Bulk Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p">Rheological measurements were performed using a rheometer (Physica MCR102, Anton Paar) under dry air conditions at 30 °C under a temperature-controlling hood (H-PTD200). The shear dependence of the viscosity was measured using a cone-plate geometry (diameter: 50 mm; angle: 1°) with a lower torque limit of 0.1 μN m, which was sufficient to estimate the zero-shear viscosity using the Cross model.</div><div class="NLM_p">The self-diffusion coefficients of the solvents (PC) and the polyanion were evaluated by pulse-field gradient nuclear magnetic resonance (PFG-NMR) spectroscopy at 60 °C using a JEOL-ECX 400 spectrometer with a bipolar pulse-pair longitudinal eddy current delay (BPP-LED) pulse sequence with sinusoidal PFG. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> The measurements targeted <sup>1</sup>H in the solvent (399.7 MHz) and <sup>19</sup>F in the polyanion (376.1 MHz).</div><div class="NLM_p">Raman spectra were acquired using a Raman spectrometer equipped with a 785 nm laser (NRS-4100, JASSO) at 60 °C and using a spectral resolution of 4.5 cm<sup>–1</sup>. The temperature was controlled using a Peltier microscope stage (TS62, INSTEC) equipped with a temperature controller (mk1000, INSTEC). The instrument was calibrated using a polypropylene standard prior to sample analysis.</div><div class="NLM_p">The ionic conductivity (σ) was measured using an impedance analyzer (VMP-3, Biologic Science Instruments) in the frequency range of 500 kHz to 50 mHz with a voltage amplitude of 10 mV at various temperatures (i.e., 25–60 °C). A platinized platinum electrode cell (CG-511B, DKK-TOA) was calibrated using a standard 0.01 M KCl aqueous solution prior to sample analysis.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The Na-ion transference number (<i>t</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>PP</sup>) was determined by the potentiostatic polarization method under anion-blocking conditions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31 ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 31 and 32">(31,32)</a> A symmetrical Na cell was assembled with a glass fiber filter (GA-55, Advantec) impregnated with the electrolyte solutions in a 2032 coin-type cell. The electrode area of the Na metal was 2.0 cm<sup>2</sup>. Electrochemical impedance measurements were performed in the frequency range of 1 MHz to 50 mHz with a voltage amplitude of 10 mV at 60 °C, using a potentiostat (Solartron Analytical, Modulab XM ECS).</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">2.4.  Surface and Interfacial Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p">In situ electrochemical quartz crystal microbalance (EQCM) measurements were performed using a potentiostat (HZ-7000, Hokuto Denko Co.) with a QCM system (QCM922A, Seiko EG&amp;G) at 60 °C. A two-electrode cell with an AT-cut 9.04 MHz quartz crystal equipped with a copper electrode (QA-A9M-CU, Seiko EG&amp;G) as the working electrode and Na metal as the combined counter and reference electrode. The frequency change of the electrode (Δ<i>f</i>) was recorded while conducting CV measurements at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> from the open-circuit voltage (OCV) to 0.1 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> at 60 °C.</div><div class="NLM_p">Atomic force microscopy (AFM) was performed using a silicon cantilever (spring constant: 42 N m<sup>–1</sup>) and an SPA400/SPI3800N microscope (Hitachi High-Tech Science Corp. Japan). The surface of the sample was observed using AFM in tapping mode to obtain the height image. The polymer layer was scratched in contact mode, and the surface was then observed in tapping mode to estimate the line profiles across the scratching hole and to determine the thickness of the polymer layer. The pure Cu electrode was soaked in a HCl solution to eliminate the oxide layer prior to observation. After performing chronoamperometry (0.1 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup> for 20 h), the Cu electrode was washed with PC and 1,2-dimethoxyethane (DME) to remove any residual electrolyte and was subsequently dried for 1 d before analysis by AFM.</div><div class="NLM_p">X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was performed using a PHI Quantera SXM ULVAC-PHI spectrometer to investigate the composition of the Cu electrode surface. The depth profile was obtained via Ar<sup>+</sup> sputtering at 4 kV. After performing linear sweep voltammetry (LSV, 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, OCV to 0.1 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) and chronoamperometry (0.1 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup> for 20 h), the Cu electrode was washed with PC and DME to remove any residual electrolyte and was subsequently dried at 80 °C for 1 d. The XPS spectra were calibrated using the C 1s peak of adventitious carbon at 284.8 eV.</div><div class="NLM_p">In situ surface-enhanced infrared absorption spectroscopy (SEIRAS) was performed using a Fourier-transform infrared (FTIR) spectrometer equipped with a HgCdTe (MCT) detector at 25 °C. A Pt-deposited Si prism and Na metal electrodes were separated by a glass fiber separator impregnated with an electrolyte in a two-electrode cell. The spectra were recorded using 128 scans in the wavenumber range of 500–3800 cm<sup>–1</sup> with an optical resolution of 4 cm<sup>–1</sup>. The SEIRAS spectra were recorded during the CV experiments at a scan rate of 1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> from OCV to 0.1 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) at 25 °C. The spectra are presented in the form of log (<i>I</i><sub>0</sub>/<i>I</i>), where <i>I</i><sub>0</sub> and <i>I</i> represent the spectra at the OCV and at the sample potential, respectively.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The surface of the Al electrode was observed using field-emission scanning electron microscopy (FE-SEM, SU8010, Hitachi High-Technologies). After performing chronoamperometry (3.8 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup> for 15 h), the Al electrode was washed with PC and DME to remove any residual electrolyte and was subsequently dried for 1 d prior to analysis by SEM.</div></div><div id="sec2_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8">2.5.  Cell Assembly</h3><div class="NLM_p">Na metal deposition/dissolution cycling was measured using a symmetrical Na cell using an automatic charge–discharge instrument (HJ1001SD8, Hokuto Denko) at 60 °C. The cycling test was performed every 2 h at a constant current of 0.2 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>.</div><div class="NLM_p">Galvanostatic charge–discharge measurements were performed on [Na metal | electrolyte | NMO] and [Na metal | electrolyte | HC] half-cells using an automatic charge–discharge instrument (HJ1001SD8, Hokuto Denko) at 60 °C. The areas of the Na metal, NMO, and HC electrodes were 2.0 cm<sup>2</sup>. The specific capacity of the cell was calculated based on the active material loading of NMO (4.7 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>) and HC (5.4 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>). The cycle performances of the NMO and HC half-cells were investigated using charge–discharge current densities of 0.1 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>. The discharge rate performance of the NMO half-cell was measured with a constant charge current density of 0.1 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>, while the discharge current density was varied from 0.1 to 10 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> every five cycles.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The bulk electrolyte ohmic resistance (<i>R</i><sub>b</sub>) and interfacial resistance (<i>R</i><sub>int</sub>) were evaluated by the electrochemical impedance method on a charged NMO half-cell. The charged NMO cathodes were subjected to 10 charge–discharge cycles, followed by charging to 3.8 V. Nyquist plots were obtained with a sinusoidal alternating voltage amplitude of 10 mV over the frequency range of 1 MHz to 50 mHz. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref66'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref66" aria-label="reference 66">(66)</a></div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10">3.1.  Characterization of the Poly(NaSTFSI-<i>r</i>-BA) Copolymer</h3><div class="NLM_p">Ion dissociation in a polyelectrolyte solution can be discussed according to Manning’s classical theory of counterion condensation, which is based on the Bjerrum length <i>l</i><sub>B</sub> = <i>e</i><sup>2</sup>/4πε<i>kT</i>, where <i>e</i> is the elementary charge, ε is the dielectric constant of the solvent, <i>kT</i> is the thermal energy, and <i>l</i><sub>B</sub> represents the range of electrostatic interactions at a fixed charge. Considering a polymer as an infinitely charged straight line, counterion condensation will occur if the linear charge density ξ = <i>l</i><sub>B</sub>/<i>d</i> (<i>d</i>: distance between charges on a polymer) exceeds the threshold ξ* = 1/|<i>z</i><sub>i</sub><i>z</i><sub>p</sub>|, where <i>z</i><sub>i</sub> is the counter-charge valence and <i>z</i><sub>p</sub> is the fixed charge valence on the polymer matrix. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> In this study, NaSTFSI was copolymerized with nonionic BA at a NaSTFSI/BA feed composition of 50:50. The free radical copolymerization technique was used to increase the distance <i>d</i> between the NaSTFSI moieties in the polyanion chain (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme 1</a>). The monomer reactivity ratios (<i>r</i>) of NaSTFSI (<i>r</i><sub>NaSTFSI</sub> = 1.24) and BA (<i>r</i><sub>BA</sub> = 0.24) in the copolymerization process were determined using the Mayo–Lewis method (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/suppl_file/am2c21557_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1 and Figure S1</a>), giving a value of <i>r</i><sub>NaSTFSI</sub> × <i>r</i><sub>BA</sub> of 0.30. The fact that <i>r</i><sub>NaSTFSI</sub> × <i>r</i><sub>BA</sub> is less than unity suggests the occurrence of random copolymerization. The <sup>1</sup>H NMR spectrum of the copolymer gives a composition of NaSTFSI/BA = 50:50, which corresponds to the feed composition (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/suppl_file/am2c21557_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2 and Figure S2</a>). In addition, the number-average molecular weight (<i>M</i><sub>n</sub>), weight-average molecular weight (<i>M</i><sub>w</sub>), and polydispersity index (<i>M</i><sub>n</sub>/<i>M</i><sub>w</sub>) were 2.0 × 10<sup>2</sup>, 5.8 × 10<sup>2</sup> kDa, and 2.9, respectively, as determined by gel permeation chromatography (GPC) with columns calibrated using polystyrene (PSt) standards (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/suppl_file/am2c21557_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>).</div><figure id="sch1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Scheme 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/medium/am2c21557_0012.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/large/am2c21557_0012.jpeg" id="gr11" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><div class="title2">Scheme 1. Synthesis of the Poly(NaSTFSI-<i>r</i>-BA) Copolymer</div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/large/am2c21557_0012.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c21557&amp;id=sch1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12">3.2.  Polyanion Entanglement and Counterion Condensation</h3><div class="NLM_p">The Huggins equation describes the scaling relationship between the viscosity and the polymer concentration and can therefore be used to understand the polymer conformation and aggregated structure of the linear polymer solution. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> This relationship is particularly complex for a polyelectrolyte solution because the electrostatic interactions between the charged polymers and the counterions affect its rheological properties. Wyatt et al. performed a comprehensive study of the scaling relationship of charged biological polymers in aqueous solutions, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35 ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 35 and 36">(35,36)</a> which showed that scaling of the zero-shear rate viscosity (η<sub>0</sub>) with the polymer concentration (<i>c</i>) can be divided into four distinct regions based on three associated critical concentrations, the overlap concentration (<i>c</i>*) and the entanglement concentrations (<i>c</i><sub>e</sub> and <i>c</i><sub>D</sub>). In the dilute region (<i>c</i> ≪ <i>c</i>*), each charged chain and counterion is homogeneously distributed, and the Debye screening length is shorter than the distance between the polyanion chains. In this case, <i>c</i>* denotes the transition at which electrostatic interactions between individual chains begin to occur. In the semidilute unentangled region (<i>c</i>* &lt; <i>c</i> &lt; <i>c</i><sub>e</sub>), the motions of different polymer chains are steadily coupled but are insufficient to cause entanglement of the polymer chains; <i>c</i><sub>e</sub> denotes the transition at which entanglement of the polymer chain occurs owing to the topological constraints at larger polymer volume fractions. In the semidilute entangled region (<i>c</i><sub>e</sub> &lt; <i>c</i> &lt; <i>c</i><sub>D</sub>), the viscosity of the polyelectrolyte solution is greater than that in the semidilute unentangled region, and <i>c</i><sub>D</sub> denotes the transition at which the electrostatic interactions are completely screened. In the case of a concentrated entangled region (<i>c</i> &gt; <i>c</i><sub>D</sub>), polyelectrolytes behave like neutral polymer solutions, and the increase in viscosity becomes more significant.</div><div class="NLM_p">The polymer concentration dependence of η<sub>0</sub> for poly(NaSTFSI-<i>r</i>-BA) in a PC solution (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a) was estimated by fitting the flow curves (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/suppl_file/am2c21557_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>) using the Cross model at 30 °C. The polyelectrolyte solution was prepared using PC as a solvent owing to its high dielectric constant (ε = 63.7 at 30 °C), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> which was expected to increase the degree of ion dissociation with a shorter Bejerrum length <i>l</i><sub>B</sub> (<i>l</i><sub>B</sub> = e<sup>2</sup>/4πε<i>kT</i>). Three distinct regions exhibiting viscosity scaling were observed in the polyelectrolyte solution: region I (<i>c</i><sub>Na</sub> &lt; 0.11 mol dm<sup>–3</sup>), region II (0.11 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> &lt; <i>c</i><sub>Na</sub> &lt; 0.56 mol dm<sup>–3</sup>), and region III (0.56 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> &lt; <i>c</i><sub>Na</sub>).</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/medium/am2c21557_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/large/am2c21557_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Zero-shear viscosity as a function of polymer concentration at 30 °C. (b) Self-diffusion coefficients of PC (<i>D</i><sub>PC</sub>) and the polyanion (<i>D</i><sub>anion</sub>) at 60 °C. (c) Peak position of the S–N–S stretching vibration mode of poly(NaSTFSI-<i>r</i>-BA) in a PC solution at 60 °C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/large/am2c21557_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c21557&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To clarify the structural behaviors of the polyanion chains, the self-diffusion coefficients of the polyanions (<i>D</i><sub>anion</sub>) and the PC (<i>D</i><sub>PC</sub>) in the polyelectrolyte solutions were evaluated at 60 °C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b). Unfortunately, the self-diffusion coefficients could not be measured in region I (<i>c</i><sub>Na</sub> &lt; 0.11 mol dm<sup>–3</sup>) owing to the presence of poorly resolved NMR signals (i.e., <sup>1</sup>H and <sup>19</sup>F). In the region II (0.11 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> &lt; <i>c</i><sub>Na</sub> &lt; 0.56 mol dm<sup>–3</sup>), <i>D</i><sub>anion</sub> significantly decreased, giving values in the order of ∼10<sup>–8</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, which are 3 orders of magnitude lower than the corresponding value of <i>D</i><sub>PC</sub>. We therefore concluded that chain crowding and overlapping likely inhibit the translational motion of the polyanion chains in this region. The further reduction in <i>D</i><sub>anion</sub> in region III (0.56 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> &lt; <i>c</i><sub>Na</sub>) to approaching values in the order of 10<sup>–9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> suggests that polyanion diffusion was strongly restricted by extensive chain entanglement.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The ionic interactions between the Na ions and the polyanion chains were further investigated by Raman spectroscopy of the S–N–S skeleton of the polyanion STFSI moiety at 60 °C (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/suppl_file/am2c21557_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>). The S–N–S stretching vibration of the bis(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide (TFSI) anion is known to be particularly sensitive to interactions with alkali metal cations in an electrolyte solution. This band shifts to higher frequencies owing to Na-ion coordination and can be attributed to various species. For example, the peak at 739–742 cm<sup>–1</sup> corresponds to a solvent-separated ion pair or a noncoordinating anion while that at 745–755 cm<sup>–1</sup> corresponds to a contact ion pair (CIP) or an ion aggregate (AGG) in which TFSI directly coordinates to the Na ions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> The STFSI moiety is a derivative of TFSI and is therefore expected to exhibit a similar high-frequency shift upon direct Na-ion coordination to produce CIP and AGG species. Accordingly, the Raman peak position was plotted as a function of <i>c</i><sub>Na</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c), which revealed that a significant peak shift to higher frequencies occurs in region II upon increasing <i>c</i><sub>Na</sub>; this peak shift leveled off in region III. These observations were attributed to the increase in counterion condensation resulting from the entanglement of polyanion chains with increasing <i>c</i><sub>Na</sub>, which resulted in the electrostatic interactions being almost fully screened at the boundary between regions II and III. These results suggest that the crossover concentrations at ∼ 0.11 and 0.56 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> could be assigned to <i>c</i><sub>e</sub> and <i>c</i><sub>D</sub>, respectively; however, the influence of <i>c</i>* remains unclear owing to an insufficient number of data points. Consequently, the structural behavior of polyanion chains was classified into three regions: a diluted and semidilute unentangled region (region I), a semidilute entangled region (region II), and a concentrated entangled region (region III).</div></div><div id="sec3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i13">3.3.  Ion Transport Properties</h3><div class="NLM_p">The ion transport properties and structural behavior of the polyanion chains were investigated at 60 °C unless specified otherwise. The ionic conductivity (σ) of poly(NaSTFSI-<i>r</i>-BA) in the PC solution as a function of <i>c</i><sub>Na</sub> and the temperature dependence of this ionic conductivity are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/suppl_file/am2c21557_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S6</a>, respectively. As shown, the ionic conductivity increased with <i>c</i><sub>Na</sub> up to ∼0.3 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> owing to an increase in the ionic carrier density. The maximum conductivity was obtained at <i>c</i><sub>Na</sub> ∼0.3 mol dm<sup>–3</sup>, regardless of the temperature. In addition, the conductivity increased with temperature, reaching 1.7 mS cm<sup>–1</sup> at 60 °C, although a slightly lower value of 0.76 mS cm<sup>–1</sup> was obtained at 25 °C. At a higher <i>c</i><sub>Na</sub> (i.e., &gt;0.3 mol dm<sup>–3</sup>), the ionic conductivity decreased owing to counterion condensation and high viscosity resulting from the extensive entanglement of polyanion chains; however, this reduction was not significant even in the concentrated entangled region (i.e., <i>c</i><sub>Na</sub> &gt; 0.56 mol dm<sup>–3</sup>), where the electrostatic interactions could be screened (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c). Unfortunately, the self-diffusion coefficient of the Na ions (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub>) could not be estimated owing to the extremely short transverse relaxation time (<i>T</i><sub>2</sub>) of Na ions; thus, the Na-ion transport behavior in the polyelectrolyte solution cannot be discussed. We therefore prepared a substitute poly(LiSTFSI-<i>r</i>-BA) copolymer in which the Na ions were exchanged with Li ions using a dialysis membrane, and the self-diffusion coefficient of the Li ions in the PC solution was investigated. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/suppl_file/am2c21557_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>, the self-diffusion of Li ions was 2 orders of magnitude higher than that of the polyanion, even in the concentrated entangled region. A similar diffusion behavior was observed for a weakly coordinating polyanion-type Li salt in a mixed carbonate solution and was thought to arise from solvent-assisted Li-ion conduction, wherein some of the Li ions diffused in a transiently solvated form away from the polyanions despite pronounced static counterion condensation; this process contributed to Li-ion conduction. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> Considering the analogy between Li and Na ions, we postulated that the Na ions diffuse away from the polyanions as a transiently solvated form, ultimately improving Na-ion conduction in the concentrated entangled region.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/medium/am2c21557_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/large/am2c21557_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) Ionic conductivities and (b) Na-ion transference numbers (<i>t</i><sub class="stack">Na</sub><sup class="stack">PP</sup>) of poly(NaSTFSI-<i>r</i>-BA) in PC solutions as a function of Na salt concentration at 60 °C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/large/am2c21557_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c21557&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In addition to the ionic conductivity, the Na-ion transference number (<i>t</i><sub>Na</sub>) is an important transport parameter of fast charge–discharge battery systems. We therefore estimated the Na-ion transference number (<i>t</i><sub class="stack">Na</sub><sup class="stack">PP</sup>) of a symmetrical Na cell via potentiostatic polarization and AC impedance measurements, according to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq 1</a>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31 ref32');
