clip: rect(3.185em, 1000.74em, 4.151em, -999.997em); top: -3.974em; left: 0em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-97"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-98" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Italic;">𝑅</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.804em; left: 0.741em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-99"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-100" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">0</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-101" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">)</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.332em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.247em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.066em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-2"><math display="inline"><msub><mrow><mi>I</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">o</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">h</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></msub><mo>=</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><mi>V</mi><mo>/</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><msub><mrow><mi>R</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">b</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">u</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">l</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">k</mi></mrow></msub><mo>+</mo><msub><mrow><mi>R</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>0</mn></mrow></msub><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></math></script>, where <i>R</i><sub>bulk</sub> is the solution resistance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> Although <i>t</i><sub class="stack">Na</sub><sup class="stack">PP</sup> represents the true Na-ion transference number only in ideal electrolytes, <i>t</i><sub class="stack">Na</sub><sup class="stack">PP</sup> can simply be defined as the current fraction carried by Na ions under anion-blocking conditions, and so is a useful parameter to characterize the transport properties of electrolyte materials in SIBs. Thus, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b shows the Na-ion transference number (<i>t</i><sub class="stack">Na</sub><sup class="stack">PP</sup>) of poly(NaSTFSI-<i>r</i>-BA) in the PC solution at 60 °C, while the impedance spectra and potentiostatic polarization curves used to determine <i>t</i><sub class="stack">Na</sub><sup class="stack">PP</sup> are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/suppl_file/am2c21557_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>. In the semidilute entangled region (i.e., <i>c</i><sub>Na</sub> &lt; 0.3 mol dm<sup>–3</sup>), <i>t</i><sub class="stack">Na</sub><sup class="stack">PP</sup> remained essentially constant at ∼0.5. MD simulations performed by McCloskey et al. showed that the intrinsic anion–anion correlations of polyanion chains in the solution can result in a low transference number. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> We therefore hypothesize that migration of the partially dissociated polyanions contributes to the anion–anion correlated motion in our system, thereby reducing <i>t</i><sub class="stack">Na</sub><sup class="stack">PP</sup> in this region. Meanwhile, <i>t</i><sub class="stack">Na</sub><sup class="stack">PP</sup> significantly increased in the concentrated entangled region (&gt;0.56 mol dm<sup>–3</sup>), exhibiting a maximum value of 0.9 at 1.3 mol dm<sup>–3</sup>. In addition, the concentrated solution at 1.3 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> can maintain a value of <i>t</i><sub class="stack">Na</sub><sup class="stack">PP</sup> &gt;0.9 at 30 and 45 °C (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/suppl_file/am2c21557_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S9 and S10</a>). It leads us to conclude that extensive entanglement of the polyanion chains suppressed both the migration and diffusion of the polyanions at higher <i>c</i><sub>Na</sub> values.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The mobility of Na ions in the concentrated poly(NaSTFSI-<i>r</i>-BA) in the PC solution (<i>c</i><sub>Na</sub> = 1.3 mol dm<sup>–3</sup>) was therefore preferentially achieved via transiently solvated Na-ion conduction along the immobilized polyanion chains, ultimately improving the Na-ion transport properties (<i>σ</i> = 1.1 mS cm<sup>–1</sup> and <i>t</i><sub class="stack">Na</sub><sup class="stack">PP</sup> = 0.9) at 60 °C.</div></div><div id="sec3_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i16" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i16">3.4.  Na Metal Deposition/Dissolution Cycling</h3><div class="NLM_p">The galvanostatic cycling of a symmetrical Na cell is useful for estimating the electrochemical compatibility of Na metal in an electrolyte to evaluate its potential for application in Na-metal batteries. In this work, cycling tests of symmetrical Na cells were performed with a deposition/dissolution current density of 0.2 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> in a 1.3 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> poly(NaSTFSI-<i>r</i>-BA)/PC solution. Each cycle test was carried out over 2 h, while the 1.0 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> NaTFSI/PC solution was used as a reference electrolyte for comparison. The polarization profiles of Na deposition/dissolution in the 1.0 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> NaTFSI/PC solution at 60 °C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>) reveals a large polarization during Na deposition/dissolution after the initial cycle, followed by cell failure after 10 h. The PC solvent is known to be electrochemically unstable against Na metal, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a> and NaTFSI has been also shown to decompose via severe side reactions with Na metal to form a highly resistive surface layer. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> As a result, Na deposition/dissolution in the 1.0 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> NaTFSI/PC system was inhibited by continuous decomposition of the electrolyte. Interestingly, the polyanion solution exhibited a low degree of polarization and a stable Na deposition/dissolution profile over 600 h, despite the use of an electrochemically unstable PC-based solution. This remarkable difference is corroborated by the time dependence of the impedance spectra of the Na symmetrical cell (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/suppl_file/am2c21557_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>). More specifically, the Nyquist plots show that the interfacial resistance (<i>R</i><sub>interface</sub>) of the 1.0 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> NaTFSI/PC solution, which includes the charge transfer resistance and the surface layer resistance, gradually increased over time, reaching 2.6 × 10<sup>4</sup> Ω after 100 h. In sharp contrast, the <i>R</i><sub>interface</sub> of the polyanion solution remained stable at a low value of 4.0 × 10<sup>2</sup> Ω over 100 h. These results demonstrate that the decomposition of the electrolyte was suppressed in the polyanion solution. In addition, the rate performance of the Na symmetrical cell containing the polyanion solution was studied at 60 °C. The highly stable Na deposition/dissolution profile could be achieved at a high current density of 0.4 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/suppl_file/am2c21557_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>). This behavior clearly shows that the polyanion solution could mitigate the Na dendrite formation even at high current densities.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/medium/am2c21557_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/large/am2c21557_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Na deposition/dissolution cycling of the symmetrical Na cell with a 1.3 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> poly(NaSTFSI-<i>r</i>-BA)/PC solution. The results for the 1.0 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> NaTFSI/PC solution are also shown for comparison. All measurements were carried out at 0.2 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> for 2 h at 60 °C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/large/am2c21557_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c21557&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i17" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i17">3.5.  Analysis of Surface Composition</h3><div class="NLM_p">An in situ EQCM was used to monitor the change in mass on the electrode surface during cyclic voltammetry. According to Sauerbrey <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq 2</a>, the change in mass (Δ<i>m</i>) of a quartz electrode is correlated with the frequency change (Δ<i>f</i>) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"></span><div class="MathJax_Display" style="text-align: left;"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-3-Frame" tabindex="0" style="text-align: left;"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-102" style="width: 5.855em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 5.287em; height: 0px; font-size: 110%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.48em, 1005.29em, 2.673em, -999.997em); top: -2.327em; left: 0em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-103"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-104"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-105"><span class="mstyle" id="MathJax-Span-106"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-107"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-108" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">Δ</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-109" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Italic;">𝑚</span></span></span></span></span><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-110" style="padding-left: 0.344em;"><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-111" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">=</span><span class="mstyle" id="MathJax-Span-112" style="padding-left: 0.344em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-113"><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-114" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">−</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-115" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Italic;">𝑘</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-116" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">Δ</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-117" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Italic;">𝑓<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.06em;"></span></span></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.332em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.247em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.066em;"></span></span></nobr></span></div><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-3"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><mi>m</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mo>−</mo><mi>k</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><mi>f</mi></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div>where <i>k</i> is a constant. Electrolyte decomposition followed by surface layer formation occurs owing to electrochemical reduction; thus, the frequency change can provide useful information concerning surface layer formation and its correlation with the subsequent electrochemical reaction.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a> shows the cyclic voltammograms and differential frequency change of the reference and polyanion electrolytes on a Cu electrode at 60 °C. A rapid increase in the frequency change (−Δ<i>f</i>) was observed in the voltammogram of the 1.0 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> NaTFSI/PC solution, with a cathodic current peak of ∼1.0 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>), and this change irreversibly increased over two cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a). As previously reported, the cathodic current corresponds to the reductive decomposition of PC and TFSI. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 43 and 44">(43,44)</a> The formation of a black surface layer was also confirmed on the electrode surface after the CV experiment (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/suppl_file/am2c21557_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13a</a>), leading us to conclude that the surface layer formed through the reductive decomposition of PC and TFSI cannot suppress the continuous electrolyte decomposition. In contrast, the cathodic current peak at ∼1.0 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) in the voltammogram of the polyanion solution became less prominent during the first cycle and completely disappeared during the second cycle, while the cathodic current flowed at a slightly more negative potential (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b). Furthermore, the color of the electrode surface did not change even after the CV experiment (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/suppl_file/am2c21557_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13b</a>), implying that electrolyte decomposition was suppressed. Moreover, the electrode surface became sticky after the CV experiment, which was attributed to the presence of a polymer (polyanion) layer that could not be removed after washing with the PC solvent. Due to the fact that the viscoelastic soft layer is not completely coupled with the oscillation of a quartz electrode, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> the observed −Δ<i>f</i> underestimates the actual frequency change arising from layer formation on the Cu electrode, making it difficult to quantitatively compare the surface layers formed on these two electrolytes. Nevertheless, the small change in −Δ<i>f</i> indicates that continuous solvent decomposition and film growth were efficiently suppressed in the polyanion solution.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/medium/am2c21557_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/large/am2c21557_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. EQCM measurements during cyclic voltammetry using a Cu-coated quartz crystal electrode; cyclic voltammograms (top) and differential frequency changes (bottom) in the (a) 1.0 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> NaTFSI/PC solution (left) and (b) 1.3 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> poly(NaSTFSI-<i>r</i>-BA)/PC solution (right) at 60 °C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/large/am2c21557_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c21557&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The polymer layer formed in the polyanion solution was further studied by AFM. In the height image of the polyanion layer on the Cu electrode in the dried state (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a), the polymer layer in the center of the image was preliminarily removed by scratching the layer with a silicon cantilever. Two representative line profiles across the scratches suggest that the thickness of the polymer layer is ∼120 nm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b). The Stokes–Einstein equation was then used to calculate the hydrodynamic radius of the polyanion, and this was estimated to be ∼5.0 nm based on <i>D</i><sub>anion</sub> and the viscosity at <i>c</i><sub>Na</sub> ∼0.11 mol dm<sup>–3</sup>, thereby suggesting that the polymer layer is a multilayer structure composed of entangled polyanion aggregates. Three-dimensional (3D) height images of the polymer layer and a pristine Cu electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c,d) clearly show an inhomogeneous surface roughness on the pristine Cu electrode, which was attributed to micropits and cracks over a height of ∼200 nm, which were formed during the rolling-mill manufacturing process. In contrast, the surface bearing a polymer layer was noticeably smoother than that of the pristine Cu electrode and exhibited a surface roughness within ∼20 nm.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/medium/am2c21557_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/large/am2c21557_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) AFM height image of the polymer layer on a Cu electrode with scratches in the center of the image. (b) Line profiles across the scratches. (c) 3D AFM height images of the pristine Cu electrode and (d) polymer layer on a Cu electrode.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/large/am2c21557_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c21557&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The above results indicate that the polyelectrolyte solution formed a dense, homogeneous polyelectrolyte layer on the electrode surface under a negative potential. The polyanion layer effectively suppresses the electrolyte decomposition, thereby stabilizing the electrolyte solution even in the presence of Na metal (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>). Moreover, a smooth polyanion surface with a high <i>t</i><sub class="stack">Na</sub><sup class="stack">PP</sup> value is expected to mitigate the undistributed Na-ion flux, leading to stable Na metal deposition/dissolution cycling. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The composition of the electrode surface layers was further characterized by XPS after cathodic polarization. Thus, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 6</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/suppl_file/am2c21557_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S14</a> show the XPS spectra of the Cu electrode against Ar etching time. Several peaks in the C 1s spectrum of the 1.0 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> NaTFSI/PC solution (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a) were assigned to the C–O, C═O, and CO<sub>3</sub> species generated during the reduction of PC on the outmost surface, while the others were attributed to the C–C and C–H of adventitious hydrocarbons. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47 ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 47 and 48">(47,48)</a> In addition, peaks corresponding to Na–F, C–F, SO<sub>2</sub>, and SO<sub><i>x</i></sub> were observed in the F 1s and S 2p spectra, respectively, whose formation was attributed to the decomposition of the TFSI anion. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49 ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49 ref50" aria-label="reference 49 and 50">(49,50)</a> It was found that etching the surface reduced the peak intensities of these organic species, whereas the peaks corresponding to inorganic species such as Na–F and sulfide became dominant. Thus, the surface layer was considered to be composed of an inner inorganic phase layer close to the electrode surface and an outer organic layer close to the electrolyte. These observations are in good agreement with the generally accepted SEI model. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> In contrast, the peaks in the XPS spectra of the polyanion solution (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b) corresponding to the PC-based reductive products (i.e., C–O, C═O, and CO<sub>3</sub>) were inconspicuous even after Ar etching, suggesting that the reduction of PC was suppressed. Furthermore, the C–F and SO<sub><i>x</i></sub> peaks at 688.9 (F 1s) and 169.0 (S 2p) eV were negligible even though the STFSI moiety, a derivative of TFSI, was expected to form similar decomposition products to the NaTFSI/PC reference solution. This result implies that continuous polyanion reduction is suppressed after the formation of the polyanion layer. No obvious change in the spectra of the polyanion solution was observed after Ar etching, and the strong peaks indicating the presence of Na–F and sulfide were attributed primarily to X-ray irradiation damage of the polyanions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> Moreover, the characteristic peaks at 284.8 (C 1s), 687.0 (F 1s), and 165.4 (S 2p) eV in the spectra of the polyanion solution were assigned to the main chain, −CF<sub>3</sub>, and −SO<sub>2</sub>– moieties of the STFSI in the residual polyanions, respectively. Although a peak corresponding to metallic Cu appeared after etching the surface for 30 s in the 1.0 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> NaTFSI/PC solution, no such peak was discernible in the spectrum of the polyanion solution, even after etching for 45 s, thereby indicating that the electrode surface was covered with a thick polyanion layer after cathodic polarization.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/medium/am2c21557_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/large/am2c21557_0007.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. XPS spectra of the Cu electrodes in the C 1s, F 1s, and S 2p ranges at various Ar etching times after cathodic polarization in (a) 1.0 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> NaTFSI/PC solution and (b) 1.3 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> poly(NaSTFSI-<i>r</i>-BA)/PC solution.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/large/am2c21557_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c21557&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_6" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i19" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i19">3.6.  Mechanism of Polyanion Layer Formation</h3><div class="NLM_p">In situ SEIRAS was performed under potential control to gain further insights into the interfacial chemistry of the polyanion solution. It should be noted that in situ SEIRAS enables the real-time monitoring of the dynamic behavior of ions and solvent molecules at the interface and thereby allows the mechanism of polyanion layer formation to be elucidated. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a> shows the SEIRAS spectra of the 1.3 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> poly(NaSTFSI-<i>r</i>-BA)/PC solution and 1.0 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> NaTFSI/PC solution; these spectra were obtained during the forward and reverse CV scans from the OCV to 0.1 V and show the differences in absorbance at a given potential in addition to that collected at the OCV. The spectrum of the 1.0 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> NaTFSI/PC solution at 1.0 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>a) shows peaks at ∼1820 and 1090 cm<sup>–1</sup>, whose intensities increased at increasingly negative potentials. These peaks were assigned to the C═O stretching and C–H bending vibration modes of the PC molecules, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> In addition, the peak corresponding to the S–N–S stretching mode of the TFSI anion overlaps at ∼1090 cm<sup>–1</sup>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> while increased intensities of the peaks at ∼1820 and 1090 cm<sup>–1</sup> may be due to the higher concentration of PC molecules close to the electrode surface upon their adsorption. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> Furthermore, the peaks at ∼1520 and 1740 cm<sup>–1</sup> showed a similar dependence on the potential, accompanied by a cathodic current flow at the reductive potential of PC (∼1.0 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a). Notably, the corresponding peaks were not observed in the attenuated total reflection (ATR)-IR spectra of the pristine electrolytes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/suppl_file/am2c21557_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15</a>); therefore, these peaks were assigned to the reductive decomposition products of PC. More specifically, the peak at ∼1520 cm<sup>–1</sup> was assigned to the CO<sub>2</sub> stretching and the CH<sub>2</sub> bending modes of the carbonate species (e.g., alkyl carbonates, C<sub>3</sub>H<sub>7</sub>OCO<sub>2</sub>Na) while that at ∼1740 cm<sup>–1</sup> was assigned to the C═C stretching mode of the dissolved propylene, which may be formed by a reductive ring–opening reaction of PC. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56 ref57'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56 ref57" aria-label="reference 56 and 57">(56,57)</a> This assignment was supported by density functional theory (DFT) simulations (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/suppl_file/am2c21557_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16</a>) of the spectra of representative carbonate species (C<sub>3</sub>H<sub>7</sub>OCO<sub>2</sub> anion, ∼1560 cm<sup>–1</sup>) and propylene (C<sub>3</sub>H<sub>6</sub>, ∼1700 cm<sup>–1</sup>). Previous ATR-IR and XPS studies further support the above assignments. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58 ref59'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58 ref59" aria-label="reference 58 and 59">(58,59)</a> Moreover, the oxidative current peaks appeared in the reverse scan at 1.25 V, accompanied by a decrease in the intensity of the propylene peak at ∼1740 cm<sup>–1</sup>. The current peak was therefore assigned to the electrooxidation of propylene. In addition, the intensities of the PC peaks at ∼1820 and 1090 cm<sup>–1</sup> decreased; however, these peaks were also observed in the OCV state, implying that the PC molecules remained adsorbed on the electrode surface. In contrast, the peak of carbonate species at ∼1520 cm<sup>–1</sup> was not observed in the OCV state, implying that these species diffused or dissolved away from the surface.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/medium/am2c21557_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/large/am2c21557_0008.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. In situ SEIRAS spectra of the (a) 1.0 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> NaTFSI/PC solution and (b) 1.3 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> poly(NaSTFSI-<i>r</i>-BA)/PC solution obtained during CV measurements between the OCV and 0.1 V. The corresponding CV profiles recorded at a scan rate of 1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> at 25 °C are also shown.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/large/am2c21557_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c21557&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In the polyelectrolyte solution, the peak at ∼1820 cm<sup>–1</sup> corresponding to C═O stretching vibration mode of PC exhibited a negative intensity at ∼1.5 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>b), indicating a reduction in the concentration of PC at the interface under negative polarization conditions. In addition, the peak at ∼1090 cm<sup>–1</sup> exhibited a high intensity at 1.5 V, suggesting that it arises from the S–N–S stretching vibration mode of the polyanion because the C–H bending vibration mode of the PC molecule at ∼1090 cm<sup>–1</sup> surely exhibits a negative intensity. It should be noted that the intensity of the polyanion peak at ∼1090 cm<sup>–1</sup> increased at increasingly negative potentials, accompanied by a decrease in the intensity of the PC peak at ∼1820 cm<sup>–1</sup>. Furthermore, the peaks at ∼1520 and 1740 cm<sup>–1</sup> corresponding to the carbonate species and propylene exhibited significantly lower intensity than those observed in the spectra of the 1.0 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> NaTFSI/PC electrolyte. We therefore propose that the dense accumulation of polyanion chains reduces the concentration of PC at the interface, suppressing its kinetic reduction. Indeed, the formation of polyelectrolyte layers under an electric field has been the subject of several previous studies. For example, Li et al. performed DFT calculations of an electric double layer in aqueous polyelectrolyte solutions, in which the charged polymer chains electrostatically overcompensated close to an oppositely charged surface. Subsequently, layered structures of charged polymer chains were formed as a result of charge inversion and overcharging. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref60'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref60" aria-label="reference 60">(60)</a> Moreover, Matsumoto <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref61'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref61" aria-label="reference 61">(61)</a> and Li <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref62'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref62" aria-label="reference 62">(62)</a> et al. observed that multiple covalently connected cationic groups in a polymeric ionic liquid undergo electrostatic self-organization with only a small entropy loss, and multivalent electrostatic interactions triggered further charge accumulation on the electrode surface. Nonaqueous polyelectrolyte solutions, in which the polyanion chains densely accumulate with the Na counterions under an electric field to form a highly concentrated polyelectrolyte layer at the interface, are expected to exhibit similar behavior. We found that the intensity of the propylene peak at ∼1740 cm<sup>–1</sup> in the reverse scan gradually decreased whereas that of the carbonate species at ∼1520 cm<sup>–1</sup> slightly increased and was also observed at the OCV. This implies that the majority of carbonate species do not diffuse or dissolve but instead adhere to the electrode surface in the polyelectrolyte solution; in contrast, they diffuse or dissolve away from the surface in the 1.0 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> NaTFSI/PC solution. More interestingly, the peak intensity of the polyanion at ∼1090 cm<sup>–1</sup> remained unchanged in the reverse scan, leading us to speculate that the accumulated polyanion chains were anchored to the interface via the carbonate species.</div><div class="NLM_p">We therefore propose detailed mechanisms describing the electrochemical behavior of the negative electrode based on the species detected by the in situ SEIRAS experiment and their correlation to the EQCM, AFM, and XPS results (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>). Upon applying a negative potential to a metal electrode in the 1.0 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> NaTFSI/PC solution (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>a), PC and TFSI are reduced at ∼1.0 V, forming an SEI layer on the electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a). The decomposition products of TFSI typically include inorganic species such as NaF, which precipitate on the electrode surface, whereas the reduction of PC results in the formation of organic species such as alkyl carbonate and propylene (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>a). These carbonate species can diffuse or dissolve away from the surface, resulting in a multicomponent SEI layer composed of inner inorganic and outer organic phases (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a). Unfortunately, the higher solubility of the components of the Na-based SEI results in a more unstable and inhomogeneous layer relative to that formed by their Li analogues. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref8" aria-label="reference 6 and 8">(6,8)</a> Consequently, the continuous decomposition of the electrolyte through minute cracks and the corresponding growth of a thicker film inhibited Na metal deposition/dissolution cycling (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>). In contrast, the polyanion chains in the polyelectrolyte solution electrochemically overcompensated close to an oppositely charged surface under negative polarization (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>b), and thus charge inversion and overcharging lead to the formation of layered structures of polyanion chains with a small entropy loss. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref60 ref61'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref60 ref61" aria-label="reference 60 and 61">(60,61)</a> The dense accumulation of these polyanion chains reduces the concentration of PC at the interface (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>b), thereby suppressing its kinetic reduction (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 4</a>b and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b). Moreover, densely accumulated polyanion chains also remained adhered to the interface, thus forming dense and homogeneous polyanion layers on the electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>). These results indicated that the carbonate species formed by the reduction of PC, as revealed by the feature at ∼1520 cm<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>b), plays a role in forming a rigid anchor through which the polyanion chains are attached to the electrode surface. It is also possible that the PC molecules are initially reduced to form a radical anion intermediate, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> which then reacts with the polyanion chains to form an anchor. The surface-tethered polyanion layer can also mitigate the undistributed Na-ion flux, leading to stable Na metal deposition/dissolution cycling (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>).</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="9" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/medium/am2c21557_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/large/am2c21557_0009.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. Proposed mechanism of the electrochemical behavior of an electrode under negative polarization in the (a) 1.0 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> NaTFSI/PC solution and (b) 1.3 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> poly(NaSTFSI-<i>r</i>-BA)/PC solution.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/large/am2c21557_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c21557&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_7" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i20" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i20">3.7.  Battery Application</h3><div class="NLM_p">A linear sweep voltammogram on the Pt electrode in the 1.3 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> poly(NaSTFSI-<i>r</i>-BA)/PC solution is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/suppl_file/am2c21557_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17</a>. The concentrated polyanion solution demonstrated high oxidative stability (&gt;4.5 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). In this work, the electrochemical performances of Na/Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> (NMO) and Na/hard carbon (HC) cells were investigated using the 1.3 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> poly(NaSTFSI-<i>r</i>-BA)/PC solution. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 9</a> shows the cycle stability of these cells at a charge–discharge current density of 0.1 mA cm<sup>–1</sup> at 60 °C. In the Na/NMO cell cycle, Na ions were extracted from the NMO electrode during the initial charging cycle according to the following reaction: Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> → Na<sub>0.18</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> + 0.26Na<sup>+</sup> + 0.26e<sup>–</sup> (theoretical capacity = 72 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), after which the following reversible reaction occurred: Na<sub>0.18</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> + 0.46Na<sup>+</sup> + 0.46e<sup>–</sup> ⇄ Na<sub>0.64</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> (theoretical capacity = 127 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref63'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref63" aria-label="reference 63">(63)</a> The Na/NMO cell containing the polyanion solution exhibited highly reversible charge–discharge cycling, with an average Coulombic efficiency of &gt;99.8% after several cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 9</a>a). The gradual capacity fading of the cell was therefore attributed to the corrosion of the Al current collector. More specifically, SEM images of the Al surface (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/suppl_file/am2c21557_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S18</a>) recorded before and after chronoamperometry at 3.8 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup> for 20 h reveal that the pristine Al surface significantly changed during chronoamperometry, indicating that the Al current collector corroded at a high potential in the polyanion solution. Al corrosion is a well-known drawback of TFSI-containing electrolytes, and it is known to deteriorate the electron-conduction pathway during cycling. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref64'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref64" aria-label="reference 64">(64)</a> We therefore assumed that the observed capacity fading of the NMO electrode was caused by Al corrosion resulting from the STFSI moieties of the polyanions, which are derivatives of TFSI.</div><figure id="fig9" data-index="10" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 9</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/medium/am2c21557_0010.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/large/am2c21557_0010.jpeg" id="gr9" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 9. Charge–discharge curves of the (a) Na/NMO cell and (b) Na/HC cell using the 1.3 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> poly(NaSTFSI-<i>r</i>-BA)/PC solution at a constant current density of 0.1 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> at 60 °C. Discharge capacities and Coulombic efficiencies of the (c) Na/NMO cell and (d) Na/HC cell on the cycle number.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/large/am2c21557_0010.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c21557&amp;id=fig9"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The sloping region in the charging curve of the Na/HC cell observed at 1.0–0.1 V corresponds to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> into the parallel HC graphene layers, whereas the plateau region below 0.1 V was attributed to Na<sup>+</sup> insertion into the HC nanopores. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref65'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref65" aria-label="reference 65">(65)</a> The Na/HC cell containing the polyanion solution also exhibited highly reversible charge–discharge cycling, despite the poor compatibility of PC-based electrolytes with HC electrodes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 9</a>b). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> A small irreversible capacity was observed during the initial charge owing to the formation of a polyanion layer with partial electrolyte decomposition, and the formation of this polyanion layer suppressed continuous electrolyte decomposition, thereby enhancing the reversible cycle performance of the cell. The initial discharge capacity of ∼200 mA g<sup>–1</sup> was relatively low owing to insufficient impregnation of the highly viscous polyanion solution into the porous electrode; however, the discharge capacity gradually increased to a constant value of ∼240 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, while the Coulombic efficiency remained essentially constant at &gt;99.9% after several cycles, thereby confirming the role of the polyanion solution in ensuring excellent long-term cycling with a small overvoltage.</div><div class="NLM_p">Subsequently, the discharge-rate capability of the Na/NMO cell with the polyanion solution was measured at a range of current densities (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 10</a>). The cell based on the 1.0 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> NaTFSI/PC system was unstable against Na metal (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>); thus, a solvate ionic liquid, [Na(G5)][TFSI], comprising a stoichiometric molar ratio of NaTFSI and G5, was used for comparison. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref67'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref67" aria-label="reference 67">(67)</a> Both electrolytes exhibited similar discharge capacities of ∼110 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> in the voltage range of 2.0–3.8 V at a low current density of 0.1 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 10</a>a,b). Upon further increasing the current density, the cell voltage reached the discharge cutoff voltage (2.0 V) before the NMO electrode was fully discharged; therefore, the discharge capacities of the cells gradually declined in both electrolytes. A remarkable difference in the discharge capacities of the cells was observed at high current densities, wherein the capacity was greatly reduced to 37 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (34% capacity retention) in the [Na(G5)][TFSI] solution at 1.0 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>, whereas the polyelectrolyte solution exhibited a significantly higher value of 94 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (85% capacity retention). In addition, the cycle behavior and the discharge rate performance of the Na/NMO cell containing the polyanion solution were studied at 30 °C (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/suppl_file/am2c21557_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S19</a>). The cell showed highly reversible charge–discharge cycling at a low current density of 0.05 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>; however, the discharge rate capability was greatly inferior to that at 60 °C.</div><figure id="fig10" data-index="11" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 10</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/medium/am2c21557_0011.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/large/am2c21557_0011.jpeg" id="gr10" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 10. Discharge curves of the Na/NMO cell with different current densities in (a) [Na(G5)][TFSI] and (b,c) poly(NaSTFSI-<i>r</i>-BA)/PC solutions with discharge cutoff voltages of 2.0 and 1.0 V at 60 °C. (d) Cycle performance of the discharge capacity with different current densities ranging from 0.1 to 10.0 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/asset/images/large/am2c21557_0011.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c21557&amp;id=fig10"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To understand the difference in the rate capabilities of these cells, we estimated the total internal resistance of each cell, which consists of the electronic and ionic resistances (<i>R</i><sub>ohm</sub>), interfacial resistance (<i>R</i><sub>interface</sub>, including the charge transfer resistances at the electrodes and the surface layer, i.e., the SEI or polyanion layer), and the Na-ion mass transport resistance (<i>R</i><sub>diffusion</sub>). Thus, <i>R</i><sub>ohm</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>interface</sub> were determined by impedance measurements of the Na/NMO cell in the charged state at 3.8 V (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/suppl_file/am2c21557_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S20</a>). The intercept on the real impedance axis at a high frequency corresponds to <i>R</i><sub>ohm</sub>, while the difference in the <i>R</i><sub>ohm</sub> value between cells represents the difference in the ionic conductivity (σ) between the [Na(G5)][TFSI] (σ = 2.4 mS cm<sup>–1</sup>) and 1.3 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> poly(NaSTFSI-<i>r</i>-BA)/PC (σ = 1.1 mS cm<sup>–1</sup>) systems at 60 °C. The depressed semicircles in the Nyquist plots represent the overlap of the charge transfer resistances at the anode and the cathode, along with the surface layer resistance. <i>R</i><sub>interface</sub> was obtained by fitting the semicircle to the equivalent circuit shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/suppl_file/am2c21557_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S20</a>. The higher <i>R</i><sub>interface</sub> of the [Na(G5)][TFSI] system was attributed to a higher SEI resistance resulting from a slight reaction with Na metal. Furthermore, <i>R</i><sub>diffusion</sub> can be calculated by <i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-4-Frame" tabindex="0" style=""><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-118" style="width: 13.469em; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.804em; left: 0.741em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-148"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-149" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">d</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-150" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">i</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-151" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">f<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.06em;"></span></span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-152" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">f<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.06em;"></span></span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-153" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">u</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-154" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">s</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-155" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">i</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-156" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">o</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-157" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">n</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-158" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">)</span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-159" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">,</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.219em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.247em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.066em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-4"><math display="inline"><msub><mrow><mi>t</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi></mrow></msub><mo>=</mo><msub><mrow><mi>R</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">o</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">h</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></msub><mo>/</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><msub><mrow><mi>R</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">o</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">h</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></msub><mo>+</mo><msub><mrow><mi>R</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">d</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">f</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">f</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">u</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">o</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">n</mi></mrow></msub><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><mo>,</mo></math></script> as reported previously, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref68'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref68" aria-label="reference 68">(68)</a> wherein the <i>t</i><sub class="stack">Na</sub><sup class="stack">PP</sup> of [Na(G5)][TFSI] was determined via potentiostatic polarization of the symmetrical Na metal cell (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/suppl_file/am2c21557_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S21</a>). The estimated values of <i>c</i><sub>Na</sub>, σ, <i>t</i><sub class="stack">Na</sub><sup class="stack">PP</sup>, <i>R</i><sub>ohm</sub>, <i>R</i><sub>interface</sub>, and <i>R</i><sub>diffusion</sub> are summarized in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table 1</a>, wherein it can be seen that the value of <i>R</i><sub>interface</sub> was twice that of the polyanion solution, thereby contributing to the superior rate capability. <i>R</i><sub>diffusion</sub> exhibited the largest difference in resistance, mainly owing to the concentration polarization of the electrolyte salts due to the difference in the Na-ion transference numbers of the [Na(G5)][TFSI] (<i>t</i><sub class="stack">Na</sub><sup class="stack">PP</sup> = 0.021) and 1.3 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> poly(NaSTFSI-<i>r</i>-BA)/PC (<i>t</i><sub class="stack">Na</sub><sup class="stack">PP</sup> = 0.9) systems. We previously demonstrated that <i>R</i><sub>diffusion</sub> strongly affects the rate performance of a battery, particularly at high current densities. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref69'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref69" aria-label="reference 69">(69)</a> The improved discharge rate capability observed herein was therefore attributed predominantly to the high values of <i>t</i><sub class="stack">Na</sub><sup class="stack">PP</sup>. During the discharge process in the polyanion solution, the discharge plateau was markedly extended to a discharge capacity of 5 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>, implying that the discharge current did not reach Na-ion diffusion-limiting conditions. We therefore further examined the rate performance with a lower discharge cutoff voltage of 1.0 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 10</a>c). Although the initial drop in voltage increased with the current density, the discharge plateau was markedly extended to a higher discharge capacity at higher current densities. In particular, a discharge capacity of 46 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (45% capacity retention) was observed at 10 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 10</a>d), thereby confirming that stable, high-rate SMBs can be produced using a concentrated polyanion solution.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. Na Salt Concentration (<i>c</i><sub>Na</sub>), Ionic Conductivity (<i>σ</i>), Na-Ion Transference Number (<i>t</i><sub class="stack">Na</sub><sup class="stack">PP</sup>), Ohmic Resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ohm</sub>), Interfacial Resistance (<i>R</i><sub>interface</sub>), and Na-Ion Mass Transport Resistance (<i>R</i><sub>diffusion</sub>) of the 1.3 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> Poly(NaSTFSI-<i>r</i>-BA)/PC and [Na(G5)][TFSI] Solutions at 60 °C<a class="ref internalNav" href="#t1fn1" aria-label="a"><sup>a</sup></a></div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" the Na metal and NMO cathode are 2.0 cm<sup>2</sup></p></div></div></div></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i23">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">This work demonstrates the preparation of a stable, high-rate sodium-metal battery, which was achieved using poly(NaSTFSI-<i>r</i>-BA) in a PC solution (NaSTFSI = sodium (4-styrenesulfonyl)-(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide, BA = butyl acrylate). An investigation into the viscosity, self-diffusion, and ionic interactions in the polyanion solution identified three distinct regions in the polyanion conformation and aggregate structure upon variation in the Na salt concentration. More specifically, these were identified as the dilute and semidilute unentangled regions, semidilute entangled regions, and concentrated entangled regions. Ion transport was achieved in the concentrated polyNa(STFSI-<i>r</i>-BA)/PC system (<i>c</i><sub>Na</sub> = 1.3 mol dm<sup>–3</sup>) by means of transiently solvated Na-ion diffusion along the immobilized polyanion chains, thereby imparting the polyanion solution high Na-ion transport properties (σ = 1.1 mS cm<sup>–1</sup> and <i>t</i><sub class="stack">Na</sub><sup class="stack">PP</sup> = 0.9) at 60 °C. We also demonstrated stable cell cycling in a symmetrical Na cell with a 1.3 mol dm<sup>–3</sup> poly(NaSTFSI-<i>r</i>-BA)/PC electrolyte, despite the electrochemical instability of the PC solvent against Na metal. Furthermore, surface characterization and interfacial dynamics revealed a dense accumulation of polyanion chains at the interface under an electric field. The partial reduction of PC molecule (i.e., carbonate species) at the interface resulted in the anchoring of the polyanion chain onto the electrode surface, thereby forming a polyanion-based protective layer that effectively suppressed continuous electrolyte decomposition and stabilized the Na metal in the polyanion solution. Finally, the assembled SMBs containing a Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> cathode exhibited an outstanding charge/discharge reversibility (Coulombic efficiency &gt;99.8%) over 200 cycles, in addition to an excellent discharge rate capability (45% capacity retention at 10 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>). This study therefore demonstrates a new electrolyte, enabling both high Na-ion transport properties and stable Na metal compatibility for the development of next-generation SMBs.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i24">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c21557?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c21557</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Mayo–Lewis plot; <sup>1</sup>H NMR spectrum; GPC curve; flow curves; Raman spectra; ionic conductivity; self-diffusion coefficient; Nyquist plots; chronoamperograms; image of a Cu surface; XPS spectra; ATR-IR spectra; calculated IR spectra; and SEM image (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/suppl_file/am2c21557_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/suppl_file/am2c21557_si_001.pdf">am2c21557_si_001.pdf (1.6 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.2c21557" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGot-2IKXj-Q-7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgDIbsqGPV7MQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXkvF2rsbk%253D&amp;md5=cfc7b2d153696dcbded22d954ff06174</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1021/cr500192f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahbi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Research Development on Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review of sodium-ion battery technol.  Future outlook of sodium-ion batteries is discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo_Iht8id70u7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgDIbsqGPV7MQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhvFSiu7bL&amp;md5=d4497339c61083b2ec2c0568261e4909</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.9b17727"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yi, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Fluorinated Ether Based Electrolyte Enabling Sodium-Metal Batteries with Exceptional Cycling Stability</span>. <i>ACS Appl.                 10.1021/acsami.9b17727</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D11%26publication_year%3D2019%26pages%3D46965-46972%26journal%3DACS%2BAppl.%2BMater.%2BInterfaces%26author%3DQ.%2BYi%26author%3DY.%2BLu%26author%3DX.%2BSun%26author%3DH.%2BZhang%26author%3DH.%2BYu%26author%3DC.%2BSun%26title%3DFluorinated%2BEther%2BBased%2BElectrolyte%2BEnabling%2BSodium-Metal%2BBatteries%2Bwith%2BExceptional%2BCycling%2BStability%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facsami.9b17727&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c21557&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facsami.9b17727&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Fluorinated Ether Based Electrolyte Enabling Sodium-Metal Batteries with Exceptional Cycling Stability</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yi, Qiang; Lu, Yao; Sun, Xiaorui; Zhang, Hua; Yu, Hailong; Sun, Chunwen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-metal batteries with conventional org. liq. electrolytes have disadvantages including dendrite deposition and safety concern.  In this work, we report a low-flammable electrolyte (NaPF6-FRE) consisting of 1 M NaPF6 in 1,2-dimethoxyethane (DME), fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC), and 1,1,1,3,3,3-hexafluoroisopropylmethyl ether (HFPM) (2:1:2, in vol. ratio).  The sym. Na and Na||Cu cells with a 1 M NaPF6-DME electrolyte absorbed in a porous separator, such as the porous glass-fiber, show very poor cycling performance.  In addn., the cell with a Na3V2(PO4)3 (NVP) cathode and 1 M NaPF6-DME electrolyte shows low Coulombic efficiency.  FEC was added into the NaPF6-DME-based electrolyte to reduce the irreversible capacity of the NVP cathode and improve the Coulombic efficiency of the cell.  However, the high reactivity of FEC with the Na electrode leads to formation of an unstable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) and large interfacial resistance, and HFPM was further added to stabilize the Na electrode surface by forming a new fluorine-contg. org. layer.  The new prepd. low-flammable electrolyte (NaPF6-FRE) with 1 M NaPF6 in DME, FEC, and HFPM (2:1:2, in vol. ratio) shows a wide electrochem. window of 5.2 V.  The Na sym. cells with this low-flammable electrolyte show superior cycling performance for 800 h with a stable voltage profile at 0.5 mA cm-2, 0.5 mA h cm-2 and 1 mA cm-2, 1 mA h cm-2, resp.  The NVP||Na cells show an excellent capacity retention of 94% after 2000 cycles and superior Coulombic efficiency of 99.9% on av. at 5 C.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp0GpDKd0RIw7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljzpvmd8F1ooQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXitFOms73I&amp;md5=8cd07959d04c27ed9b60b6af1ad44e1f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1021/acsaem.0c02510"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High-Performance Sodium Metal Batteries with Sodium–Bismuth Alloy Anode</span>. <i>ACS Applied Energy Materials</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>3</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">12607</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">12612</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-Performance Sodium Metal Batteries with Sodium-Bismuth Alloy Anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Guopeng; Li, Nianwu; Sun, Chunwen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">For the application of sodium batteries, one challenge is the irregular deposition and dendrite growth during the cycling process.  Herein, it is reported that spontaneous alloying reactions can occur by the mech. mixing and pressing processes of the sodium metal and semimetal bismuth powder, which can regulate the deposition of sodium ions.  The improvement of the surface tension and affinity to sodium ions of the metal electrode can inhibit the dendrite growth.  The alloy anode can be used not only in carbonate-based electrolytes but also in the flame-retardant electrolyte.  It is demonstrated that the Na-Bi alloy electrode in a carbonate electrolyte keeps stable cyclability with a much lower overpotential of 20 mV for more than 1200 h.  The voltage hysteresis can be effectively reduced by using the Na-Bi alloy anode.  The Na3V2(PO4)3 electrode with a flame-retardant electrolyte shows a Coulombic efficiency of 99.9% at 585 mA g-1 during long cycles.  It still shows an impressive capacity retention of 95%.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp2SPVn5UIsfbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljzpvmd8F1ooQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXisFSntrrJ&amp;md5=d1736689b01e28a523838b89415a3cec</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.8b09991"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yi, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Durable Sodium Battery with a Flexible Na3Zr2Si2PO12–PVDF–HFP Composite Electrolyte and Sodium/Carbon Cloth Anode</span>. <i>ACS Appl.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Durable Sodium Battery with a Flexible Na3Zr2Si2PO12-PVDF-HFP Composite Electrolyte and Sodium/Carbon Cloth Anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yi, Qiang; Zhang, Wenqiang; Li, Shaoqing; Li, Xinyuan; Sun, Chunwen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">High-performance electrolytes and electrode materials play a crit. role in advanced sodium-ion batteries with higher energy densities.  In this work, we prepd. a poly(Me methacrylate)-filled composite electrolyte (named as GHSE) by in situ polymn. of Me methacrylate in the porous Na3Zr2Si2PO12-polymer vinylidene fluoride-hexafluoropropylene composite membrane for the first time.  The GHSE membrane exhibits a high ionic cond. (2.78 × 10-3 S cm-1), wide electrochem. window (∼4.9 V), high Na+ ion transference no. (∼0.63), good thermal stability and flexibility, as well as smaller interfacial resistance.  Moreover, a composite Na/C anode was prepd., which shows good dendrite suppression ability.  The full cell Na0.67Ni0.23Mg0.1Mn0.67O2 |GHSE|Na/C exhibits excellent rate capability with an initial discharge capacity of 96 mAh g-1 even at a higher c.d. of 192 mA g-1 and excellent cyclability for 600 cycles.  These results suggest that the GHSE and Na/C anode are promising electrolyte and anode materials for Na-ion batteries, resp.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq2gIV4a1ohrbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljzpvmd8F1ooQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXhslGqsLzI&amp;md5=6c6b7f2e38d6b2679a43513238f4a2b6</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1039/c4ta04428b"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ponrouch, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Monti, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Boschin, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Steen, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Johansson, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Palacín, M.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Non-aqueous electrolytes for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ponrouch, A.; Monti, D.; Boschin, A.; Steen, B.; Johansson, P.; Palacin, M. R.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">22-42</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The 1st review of the various electrolytes currently used and developed for Na-ion batteries (SIBs), both in terms of materials and concepts, is presented.  In contrast to the Li-ion battery (LIB), which is a mature technol. for which a more or less unanimously accepted std. electrolyte exists: 1 M LiPF6 in EC/DMC, the electrolyte of choice for SIBs has not yet fully conformed to a std.  This is true for both materials: salts, solvents, or additives, and concept, using the main track of org. solvents or aiming for other concepts.  SIB research currently prospers, benefitting from using know-how gained from 30 years of LIB R and D.  Here the currently employed electrolytes are emphasized and their effects on practical SIB performance are outlined, scrutinizing the rationale for specific choices made, salts, solvents, additives, concns., etc. for each specific cell set-up and usage conditions.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpb_d9E3Qe0wLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgqkb_KdWdj8g"> &gt;&gt;         </div><div class="casAbstract">It is often stated that formation of a functional solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) in sodium ion batteries is hampered by the higher soly. of SEI components such as sodium salts in comparison to the lithium analogs.  In order to investigate these phenomena, SEI formation and functionality, as well as cell self-discharge, are studied for the sodium ion system with comparative expts. on the equiv. lithium ion system.  By conducting a set of expts. on carbonaceous anodes, the impact of SEI dissoln. is tested.  The results show that the SEI layer in sodium ion cells is inferior to that in lithium ion counterparts with regards to self-discharge; sodium cells show a loss in capacity at a dramatic rate as compared to the lithium counterparts when they are stored at sodiated and lithiated states, resp., for a long time with no external applied current or potential.  Also, synchrotron-based hard XPS measurements indicate that the major factor leading to increased self-discharge is dissoln. of significant parts of the sodium-based SEI.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Highly Reversible Room-Temperature Sodium Metal Anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Seh, Zhi Wei; Sun, Jie; Sun, Yongming; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Central Science</span>
