        </div><div class="casAbstract">Owing to its low cost and high natural abundance, sodium metal is among the most promising anode materials for energy storage technologies beyond lithium ion batteries.  However, room-temp. sodium metal anodes suffer from poor reversibility during long-term plating and stripping, mainly due to formation of nonuniform solid electrolyte interphase as well as dendritic growth of sodium metal.  Herein it is reported for the first time that a simple liq. electrolyte, sodium hexafluorophosphate in glymes (mono-, di-, and tetraglyme), can enable highly reversible and nondendritic plating-stripping of sodium metal anodes at room temp.  High av. Coulombic efficiencies of 99.9% were achieved over 300 plating-stripping cycles at 0.5 mA cm-2.  The long-term reversibility was found to arise from the formation of a uniform, inorg. solid electrolyte interphase made of sodium oxide and sodium fluoride, which is highly impermeable to electrolyte solvent and conducive to nondendritic growth.  As a proof of concept, a room-temp. sodium-sulfur battery is demonstrated using this class of electrolytes, paving the way for the development of next-generation, sodium-based energy storage technologies.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrdqXOWzEW74bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgSev0shGbEZQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhvVGmtrfL&amp;md5=6155e3c781a24b8340081c2ad4444616</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1002/inf2.12023"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lin, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chou, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Recent Research Progresses in Ether- and Ester-Based Electrolytes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>InfoMat</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>1</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">376</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">389</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Owing to the natural abundance and low cost of sodium resources, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have drawn considerable attention for state-of-the-art power storage devices over the last few years.                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Elucidation of the Solid Electrolyte Interphase Formation Mechanism in Micro-Mesoporous Hard-Carbon Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Alptekin, Hande; Au, Heather; Olsson, Emilia; Cottom, Jonathon; Jensen, Anders CS; Headen, Thomas F.; Cai, Qiong; Drew, Alan J.; Crespo Ribadeneyra, Maria; Titirici, Maria-Magdalena</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The microstructure of hard carbons can be designed to maximize their performance as anodes for sodium-ion batteries.  However, the nature of the electrolyte is also decisive in the capacity and long-term stability.  Here, hard carbons with a tailored bimodal pore network of internal micropores interconnected through mesopores are studied as sodium-ion battery anodes.  The evolution of their solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) is analyzed in three different electrolytes (NaPF6 in an ether-based solvent, and NaPF6 or NaClO4 in a carbonate-based system).  Combining expts. with d. functional theory calcns., it is proposed that formation of the SEI is mainly controlled by the decompn. of the salt anion.  This process occurs through the intermediate functionalization of the carbon surface by the decompd. anion fragments.  It is suggested that the innermost SEI sub-layer governs the performance and long-term stability of the anode.  While the presence of a fluorine-contg. salt appears to have a detg. role in the SEI stability, the electrochem. decompn. of carbonate-based solvents is detrimental for the long-term stability as the interfacial resistance increases.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The continued search for routes to improve the power and energy d. of lithium ion batteries for elec. vehicles and consumer electronics has resulted in significant innovation in all cell components, particularly in electrode materials design.  In this Review, we highlight an often less noted route to improving energy d.: increasing the Li+ transference no. of the electrolyte.  Turning to Newman's original lithium ion battery models, we demonstrate that electrolytes with modestly higher Li+ transference nos. compared to traditional carbonate-based liq. electrolytes would allow higher power densities and enable faster charging (e.g., &gt;2C), even if their cond. was substantially lower than that of conventional electrolytes.  Most current research in high transference no. electrolytes (HTNEs) focuses on ceramic electrolytes, polymer electrolytes, and ionomer membranes filled with nonaq. solvents.  We highlight a no. of the challenges limiting current HTNE systems and suggest addnl. work on promising new HTNE systems, such as "solvent-in-salt" electrolytes, perfluorinated solvent electrolytes, nonaq. polyelectrolyte solns., and solns. contg. anion-decorated nanoparticles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrUAnZcDnmkz7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgSev0shGbEZQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhs1Wgt7jN&amp;md5=75659d6fa44611ddbdd4039369560cc6</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1002/adma.202100962"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dong, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Poly(ionic liquid)s Containing Alkoxy Chains and Bis(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide Anions as Highly Adhesive Materials</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Adhesive materials have wide applications in diverse fields, but the development of a novel and multipurpose adhesive is a great challenge.  This study demonstrates that conventional poly(ionic liq.)s (PILs) can be designed as highly efficient adhesives by simply introducing alkoxy moieties into the cationic backbone of PILs contg. bis(trifluoromethanesulfonimide) (TFSI-) anions.  The incorporated flexible alkoxy chain not only reduces the glass transition temp. of PILs but also endows these materials with strong hydrogen bonding interactions, which, together with the unique electrostatic interaction of the PILs, simultaneously contributes to a high cohesive energy and interfacial adhesive energy.  Consequently, these alkoxy PILs are highly adhesive on various substrates such as glass, ceramic, stainless steel, aluminum, and polymers, in contrast to the nonadhesive behavior of conventional PILs.  Photosensitive or electronically conductive composite adhesives are fabricated by virtue of the compatibility between ionic liqs. and carbon nanotubes or silver nanofibers.                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Poly(ethylene oxide) as a alternative host materials for Li+-conducting solid polymer electrolytes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Mindemark, Jonas; Lacey, Matthew J.; Bowden, Tim; Brandell, Daniel</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Progress in Polymer Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The bulk of the scientific literature on Li-conducting solid (solvent-free) polymer electrolytes (SPEs) for applications such as Li-based batteries is focused on polyether-based materials, not least the archetypal poly(ethylene oxide) (PEO).  A significant no. of alternative polymer hosts have, however, been explored over the years, encompassing materials such as polycarbonates, polyesters, polynitriles, polyalcs. and polyamines.  These display fundamentally different properties to those of polyethers, and might therefore be able to resolve the key issues restricting SPEs from realizing their full potential, for example in terms of ionic cond., chem. or electrochem. stability and temp. sensitivity.  It is further interesting that many of these polymer materials complex Li ions less strongly than PEO and facilitate ion transport through different mechanisms than polyethers, which is likely crit. for true advancement in the area.  In this review, &gt;30 years of research on these 'alternative' Li-ion-conducting SPE host materials are summarized and discussed in the perspective of their potential application in electrochem. devices, with a clear focus on Li batteries.  Key challenges and strategies forward and beyond the current PEO-based paradigm are highlighted.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpu9JQKu0cWerVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljKffFpcuKtBQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXit1alsL0%253D&amp;md5=a52acc9b75b5b026e72e835e8eada998</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2015.03.228"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Boschin, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Johansson, P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Characterization of NaX (X: TFSI, FSI) – PEO Based Solid Polymer Electrolytes for Sodium Batteries</span>. <i>Electrochim. While for the most dil. systems (n = 20) the two SPE families show similar ionic conductivities in the entire temp. range studied (273-343 K), for n = 6 and n = 9 they differ significantly; at room temp., the NaFSI based SPEs show lower ionic conductivities than the NaTFSI based analogs.                         cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Relationship between Conductivity, Ion Diffusion, and Transference Number in Perfluoropolyether Electrolytes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chintapalli, Mahati; Timachova, Ksenia; Olson, Kevin R.; Mecham, Sue J.; Devaux, Didier; DeSimone, Joseph M.; Balsara, Nitash P.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Macromolecules (Washington, DC, United States)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3508-3515</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">MAMOBX</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Connecting continuum-scale ion transport properties such as cond. and cation transference no. to microscopic transport properties such as ion dissocn. and ion self-diffusivities is an unresolved challenge in characterizing polymer electrolytes.  Better understanding of the relationship between microscopic and continuum scale transport properties would enable the rational design of improved electrolytes for applications such as lithium batteries.  We present measurements of continuum and microscopic ion transport properties of nonflammable liq. electrolytes consisting of binary mixts. of lithium bis(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide (LiTFSI) and perfluoropolyethers (PFPE) with different end groups: diol, di-Me carbonate, ethoxy-diol, and ethoxy-dimethyl carbonate.  The continuum properties, cond. and cation transference no., were measured by ac impedance spectroscopy and potentiostatic polarization, resp.  The ion self-diffusivities were measured by pulsed field gradient NMR spectroscopy (PFG-NMR), and a microscopic cation transference no. was calcd. from these measurements.  The measured ion self-diffusivities did not reflect the measured conductivities; in some cases, samples with high diffusivities exhibited low cond.  We introduce a nondimensional parameter, β, that combines microscopic diffusivities and cond.  We show that β is a sensitive function of end-group chem.  In the ethoxylated electrolytes, β is close to unity, the value expected for electrolytes that obey the Nernst-Einstein equation.  In these cases, the microscopic and continuum transference nos. are in reasonable agreement.  PFPE electrolytes devoid of ethoxy groups exhibit values of β that are significantly lower than unity.  In these cases, there is significant deviation between microscopic and continuum transference nos.  We propose that this may be due to electrostatic coupling of the cation and anion or contributions to the NMR signal from neutral ion pairs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpxUzpbtJYXW7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0licCvS167zxdg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XmvFGjsrg%253D&amp;md5=066353b09750183978dee493395f0bfc</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1039/c7sm00794a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Timachova, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chintapalli, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Olson, K.                         cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Mechanism of ion transport in perfluoropolyether electrolytes with a lithium salt</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Timachova, Ksenia; Chintapalli, Mahati; Olson, Kevin R.; Mecham, Sue J.; DeSimone, Joseph M.; Balsara, Nitash P.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Soft Matter</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Perfluoropolyethers (PFPEs) are polymer electrolytes with fluorinated carbon backbones that have high flash points and have been shown to exhibit moderate conductivities and high cation transference nos. when mixed with lithium salts.  Ion transport in four PFPE electrolytes with different end groups was characterized by differential scanning calorimetry (DSC), ac impedance, and pulsed-field gradient NMR (PFG-NMR) as a function of salt concn. and temp.  In spite of the chem. similarity of the electrolytes, salt diffusion coeffs. measured by PFG-NMR and the glass transition temp. measured by DSC appear to be uncorrelated to ionic cond. measured by ac impedance.  We calc. a non-dimensional parameter, β, that depends on the salt diffusion coeffs. and ionic cond.  We also use the Vogel-Tammann-Fulcher relationship to fit the temp. dependence of cond.  We present a linear relationship between the prefactor in the VTF fit and β; both parameters vary by four orders of magnitude in our exptl. window.  Our anal. suggests that transport in electrolytes with low dielec. consts. (low β) is dictated by ion hopping between clusters.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrY8ARCWk6RfbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0licCvS167zxdg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhtFGhs7jN&amp;md5=ad0f56a7c10be48775f9e0b7df6c7dbc</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1038/s41563-022-01319-w"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Armand, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Forsyth, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Cationic Polymer-in-Salt Electrolytes for Fast Metal Ion Conduction and Solid-State Battery Applications</span>. <i>Nat.                         cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Cationic polymer-in-salt electrolytes for fast metal ion conduction and solid-state battery applications</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chen, Fangfang; Wang, Xiaoen; Armand, Michel; Forsyth, Maria</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">21</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1175-1182</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NMAACR</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Portfolio</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Polymer electrolytes provide a safe soln. for future solid-state high-energy-d. batteries.  Materials that meet the simultaneous requirement of high ionic cond. and high transference no. remain a challenge, in particular for new battery chemistries beyond lithium such as Na, K and Mg.  Herein, we demonstrate the versatility of a polymeric ionic liq. (PolyIL) as a polymer solvent to achieve this goal for both Na and K.  Using mol. simulations, we predict and elucidate fast alkali metal ion transport in PolyILs through a structural diffusion mechanism in a polymer-in-salt environment, facilitating a high metal ion transference no. simultaneously.  Exptl. validation of these computationally designed Na and K polymer electrolytes shows good ionic conductivities up to 1.0 x 10-3 S cm-1 at 80 °C and a Na+ transference no. of 0.57.  An electrochem. cycling test on a Na|2:1 NaFSI/PolyIL|Na sym. cell also demonstrates an overpotential of 100 mV at a c.d. of 0.5 mA cm-2 and stable long-term Na plating/stripping performance of more than 100 h.                         cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Poly(Ionic Liquid)s-in-Salt Electrolytes with Co-coordination-Assisted Lithium-Ion Transport for Safe Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Xiaoen; Chen, Fangfang; Girard, Gaetan M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Polymer electrolytes are seen as key components for safe, high-energy-d. all-solid-state lithium (Li) batteries, given their appropriate mech. properties along with the absence of volatile and flammable solvents.  Here, we report a class of solvent-free polymer electrolytes by using poly(diallyldimethylammonium) bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide (PDADMA FSI) and a high content of Li bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide (LiFSI) salt.  A homogeneous mixt., with a Li+ ion transference no. of 0.56, is found at a 1:1.5 mol ratio of PDADMA FSI:LiFSI, whereas cryst. phases are obsd. at higher LiFSI contents.  Mol. dynamics (MD) simulations indicate a co-coordination of the FSI anion with both the Li+ and the polymer backbone, and this co-coordination is maximized at 1:1.5 mol ratio, which facilitates Li+ transport.  The optimized electrolyte has been demonstrated to support long-term stable Li-metal sym. cycling as well as Li-metal battery cycling (Li | LiFePO4 and Li | Li1/3Ni1/3Mn1/3CoO2) at elevated temp., with areal capacity higher than 1.1 mAh cm-2.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoRjoujTeVgnrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lieD-Vlwd17iQ"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Single ion conducting sodium ion batteries enabled by a sodium ion exchanged poly(bis(4-carbonyl benzene sulfonyl)imide-co-2,5-diamino benzesulfonic acid) polymer electrolyte</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Pan, Qiyun; Li, Zhong; Zhang, Wenchao; Zeng, Danli; Sun, Yubao; Cheng, Hansong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Solid State Ionics</span>

                        cas_cit24R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">24</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium ion dynamics in a sulfonate based ionomer system studied by 23Na solid-state nuclear magnetic resonance and impedance spectroscopy</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Pope, Cameron R.; Romanenko, Konstantin; MacFarlane, Douglas R.; Forsyth, Maria; O'Dell, Luke A.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A poly(2-acrylamido-2-methyl-1-propane-sulfonate) (PAMPS) ionomer contg. both Na and quaternary ammonium cations functionalized with an ether group, was characterized in terms of its thermal properties, ionic cond. and Na ion dynamics.                         cas_cit25R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">25</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Synthesis of Sodium Poly[4-styrenesulfonyl(trifluoromethylsulfonyl)imide]-co-ethylacrylate Solid Polymer Electrolytes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Jiaye; Zhu, Haijin; Wang, Xiaoen; Armand, Michel; MacFarlane, Douglas R.; Forsyth, Maria</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-based batteries are being considered to replace Li-based batteries for the fabrication of large-scale energy storage devices.  One of the main obstacles is the lack of safe and conductive solid Na-ion electrolytes.  A Na-ion polymer based on the (4-styrenesulfonyl(trifluromethylsulfonyl)) imide anion, Na[STFSI], has been prepd. by a radical polymn. process and its conductive properties detd.  In addn., a no. of multi-component polymers were synthesized by co-reaction of two monomers: Na[STFSI] and Et acrylate (EA) at different ratios.  The structural and phase characterizations of the polymers were probed by various techniques (DSC, TGA, NMR, GPC, Raman, FTIR and Impedance spectroscopy).  Comparative studies with blends of the homopolymers Na[PSTFSI] and poly(ethylacrylate) (PEA) have also been performed.  The polymers are all thermally stable up to 300° and the ionic cond. of EA copolymers and EA blends are about 1-3 orders of magnitude higher than that of Na[PSTFSI].  The highest cond. measured at 100° was found for Na[PSTFSI-blend-5EA] at 7.9 × 10-9 S cm-1, despite being well below its Tg.  Vibrational spectroscopy indicates interaction between Na+ and the EA carbonyl groups, with a concomitant decrease in the sulfonyl interaction, facilitating Na+ motion, as well as lowering Tg.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoCbPNjLxTgo7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lieD-Vlwd17iQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXktlGqs74%253D&amp;md5=040824301bec66f25bdf6965d0dc7da0</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref26"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">26</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit26" data-doi="10.1021/acsenergylett.6b00724"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Buss, H.                         cas_cit26R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">26</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nonaqueous Polyelectrolyte Solutions as Liquid Electrolytes with High Lithium Ion Transference Number and Conductivity</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Buss, Hilda G.; Chan, Sophia Y.; Lynd, Nathaniel A.; McCloskey, Bryan D.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Energy Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Although the small-mol. lithium salt solns. currently employed in lithium-ion batteries display high ionic cond., σ, most of the initial current is carried by the electrochem. inactive anion, resulting in concn. polarization of the salt during cycling.  Liq. electrolytes with high Li+ transference no., t+, and high σ could improve battery performance by limiting these concn. gradients.  We propose lithium-neutralized polyanions in soln. as an intriguing strategy to attain both high t+ and high σ at room temp. and validate the approach using a model system.  Pulsed field gradient NMR spectroscopy (PFG-NMR) is used to attain self-diffusion coeffs. of the ions and hence t+.  Cond. calcd. from PFG-NMR showed good agreement with cond. measured using a cond. meter.  Although the polyelectrolyte solns. exhibit maxima in cond. at around 0.5 M Li+ (1.5 mS/cm), t+ continuously increases with concn., achieving 0.98 at 1.0 M Li+ in solns. contg. the highest mol. wt. polyanion studied.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpOqTpDuygsG7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljhnTTJEsZoRQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhtlCgsLc%253D&amp;md5=c3e5a2cd3c105240c0b018692cd5bbba</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref27"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">27</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit27" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.9b05219"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dewing, B.                         cas_cit27R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">27</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemically Stable, High Transference Number Lithium Bis(malonato)borate Polymer Solution Electrolytes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Dewing, Beth L.; Bible, Nicholas G.; Ellison, Christopher J.; Mahanthappa, Mahesh K.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Implementation of next-generation Li-ion batteries based on high-voltage cathodes requires the development of new electrolytes that are stable over wide electrochem. potential windows.  High lithium-ion transference no. (tLi+) electrolytes, in which most of the ionic current is carried by Li+, offer addnl. opportunities to improve the efficiencies of such batteries by minimizing unproductive anion motion.  Herein, we report the synthesis and characterization of soln. electrolytes based on poly(lithium bis(nonenylmalonato)borate) (P(LiBNMB)) with mol. wts. Mn = 2.7-55 kg/mol and dispersities D = Mw/Mn = 1.6-3.2.  Cyclic voltammetry studies reveal that propylene carbonate (PC) solns. of P(LiBNMB) are electrochem. stable up to 5.2 V (vs. Li/Li+).  Electrochem. impedance spectroscopy (EIS) studies indicate that the total ionic conductivities of these P(LiBNMB)/PC soln. electrolytes with [Li+] ≈ 0.10 M are nearly Mn-invariant with σ = 0.31-0.39 mS/cm.  Complementary ion diffusivity measurements by 1H and 7Li NMR diffusion ordered spectroscopy (DOSY) and potentiostatic polarization also reveal that they exhibit high tLi+ = 0.77-0.98.  Thus, semidilute solns. of oligomeric salts comprising as few as 21 monomer units unexpectedly display tLi+ &gt; 0.90 with σ = 0.34 mS/cm.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqadNSmzGlHW7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljhnTTJEsZoRQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXnvFOhtLw%253D&amp;md5=df5a38d23cebce222e98fd0639c1e652</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref28"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">28</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit28" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.1c25067"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kondou, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sakashita, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hashimoto, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dokko, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Watanabe, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ueno, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Li-Ion Transport and Solvation of a Li Salt of Weakly Coordinating Polyanions in Ethylene Carbonate/Dimethyl Carbonate Mixtures</span>. <i>ACS Appl. (tLi) have attracted significant attention for the improvement of the rapid charge-discharge performance of Li-ion batteries (LIBs).  Nonaq. polyelectrolyte solns. exhibit high tLi upon immobilization of the anion on a polymer backbone.  However, the transport properties and Li-ion solvation in these media are not fully understood.  Here, we investigated the Li salt of a weakly coordinating polyanion, poly[(4-styrenesulfonyl)(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)amide] (poly(LiSTFSA)), in various ethylene carbonate and di-Me carbonate mixts. This was attributed to a unique conduction phenomenon resulting from the faster diffusion of transiently solvated Li ions along the interconnected aggregates of polyanion chains.  A Li/LiFePO4 cell using such an electrolyte demonstrated improved rate capability.                         cas_cit30R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">30</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The Stejskal-Tanner equation generalized for any gradient shape-an overview of most pulse sequences measuring free diffusion</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sinnaeve, Davy</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Concepts in Magnetic Resonance, Part A: Bridging Education and Research</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The widely known Stejskal-Tanner (ST) equation is central to any diffusion NMR or so-called pulsed gradient spin-echo expt., describing the signal attenuation due to loss of coherence caused by diffusion as a function of the exptl. parameters.  What is less widely known is that the equation itself is not invariable when applying different pulse sequences or different gradient pulse shapes and should thus be modified accordingly.  This concept is not new, but nevertheless experimentalists applying diffusion NMR in their research often overlook or are completely oblivious to this fact.  In this article, the derivation of the ST equation through the Bloch-Torrey equations is discussed in detail, followed by a discussion of the most basic NMR expts. that measure free diffusion.  This derivation is performed here in a novel way, leading to expressions for the ST equation that do not assume any gradient shape beforehand, leaving only a few parameters that are detd. solely by the gradient pulse shape.  This new approach to presenting the ST equation increases awareness of its dependence on gradient shape.  Moreover, it relieves designers of future diffusion NMR pulse sequences of struggling with the cumbersome task of deriving and reporting the equation for each gradient shape sep.  A quick ref. table of the ST equation for the basic diffusion NMR pulse sequences for any gradient shape is given, as well as an overview for most other diffusion NMR pulse sequences described in the literature. © 2012 Wiley Periodicals, Inc.     </div><div class="casAbstract">Straightforward methods based on d.c. techniques are described which permit reliable evaluation of both the total cond. and transference nos. of binary polymer electrolytes.  In theory, under restricted conditions, transference nos. may be obtained from polarization data on cells of the type M(s)|M+X-(polymer)|M(s).  In practice such measurements are frequently complicated by electrode resistances.  Here methods are demonstrated for reliably extg. transference nos. in the presence of such effects; measurements are presented for PEO-LiCF3SO3 (9:1) electrolyte as a function of temp.     </div><div class="casAbstract">cf. C. A. 34, 303.8.  A simple, reasonable modification of H.'s previous theoretical treatment of the viscosity of dil. solns. of long-chain mols. leads to the equation ηsp/c = (ηsp/c)c=0 (1 + k'ηsp) for the initial concn. dependence of the viscosity.  This is identical with an equation arrived at empirically by Schulz and Blaschke (C. A. 36, 17.9).  The const. k' is characteristic of a given solute-solvent system; it is the same for solns., in a given solvent, of different members of a polymer-homologous series, however.  At low concns. (such as assumed in the theoretical derivation), this relation is equiv. to ηsp/c = (ηsp/c)2c=0 + k'(ηsp/c)2c=o C.  Equations proposed by Baker (C. A. 7, 4069), by Fikentscher and Mark (C. A. 24, 1010), by de Jong, Kruyt and Lens (C. A. 27, 458) and by Martin all reduce to this same limiting equation, with k' different for different systems.  Equations proposed by Arrhenius (Medd. Vetenskapsakad. Nobelinst. 4, 13 (1916)), by Hess and Philippoff (C. A. 31, 4873.3) and by Bred´ee and de Booys (C. A. 31, 4562.6) likewise reduce to this form, but with k' having the same value for all systems.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoJoLGljJYg0rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgHYsKnYQZMmg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaH3sXhtFE%253D&amp;md5=74388494727bfcb41a16302efe5d240c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref35"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">35</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit35" data-doi="10.1002/app.31093"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wyatt, N.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The viscosity as a function of concn. for xanthan gum in both salt-free soln. and in 50 mM NaCl is measured and compared with a scaling theory for polyelectrolytes.  In general, the zero shear rate viscosity and the degree of shear thinning increase with polymer concn.  In addn., shear thinning was obsd. in the dil. regime in both solvents.  In salt-free soln., four concn. regimes of viscosity scaling and three assocd. crit. concns. were obsd. (c* ≈ 70 ppm, ce ≈ 400 ppm, and cD ≈ 2000 ppm).  In salt soln., only three concn. regimes and two crit. concns. were obsd. (c* ≈ 200 ppm and ce ≈ 800 ppm).  In the presence of salt, the polymer chain structure collapses and occupies much less space resulting in higher values of the crit. concns.                         cas_cit36R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">36</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Increasing viscosity in entangled polyelectrolyte solutions by the addition of salt</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wyatt, Nicholas B.; Gunther, Casey M.; Liberatore, Matthew W.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Polymer</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2011</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">52</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The viscosity of several polyelectrolytes is measured in both salt free solns. and solns. in the high salt limit.  At low polymer concns., the zero shear rate viscosity decreases as much as 100-fold upon addn. of a monovalent salt, namely NaCl.  However, as polymer concn. increases, the viscosity difference between polymer in salt free and in monovalent salt soln. diminishes.  Further, the zero shear rate viscosity becomes independent of added monovalent salt at the crit. polyelectrolyte concn. cD.  Above cD, the addn. of monovalent salt increases the zero shear rate viscosity of the entangled polyelectrolyte solns.  The viscosity increase agrees with viscosity scaling theory for polyelectrolytes in the entangled regime.  Polyelectrolytes exhibiting an increase in viscosity above cD in the presence of monovalent salt include three natural anionic polyelectrolytes (xanthan, carrageenan, welan), one synthetic anionic polyelectrolyte (hydrolyzed polyacrylamide), and one natural cationic polyelectrolyte (chitosan).  Generally, these polyelectrolytes are relatively high mol. wt. (&gt;1 M Da), which makes cD exptl. accessible (e.g., cD = 0.2 wt% for xanthan).  The magnitude of the viscosity increase is as high as 300% for xanthan and nearly independent of monovalent salt concn. in the high salt limit.  The increase in viscosity in monovalent salt soln. and magnitude of cD appear to be heavily influenced by the mol. characteristics of the polymers such as monomer wt., mol. structure, and chain conformation.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrG-L6ZP-QODbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgHYsKnYQZMmg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXlvVWjtrg%253D&amp;md5=63b181e00b6948d8646d5dd27deded1a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref37"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">37</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit37" data-doi="10.1021/j100664a019"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Payne, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Theodorou, I.     </div><div class="casAbstract">The dielec. properties of ethylene carbonate and propylene carbonate were studied by a pulse reflection technique and a.c. measurements at 1-9000 MHz.  Equil. dielec. consts. for the pure liqs. and mixts. with other liq. dielecs. are consistent with the absence of specific intermol. forces.  The dipole relaxation process is described by the Debye equations with relaxation times in the psec region at room temp. and the nsec region for supercooled propylene carbonate at -78°.  The apparent limiting high-frequency dielec. const. in both liqs. is approx. 10, suggesting the existence of a 2nd dispersion region at &gt;9000 MHz.  The relaxation times and the viscosity of propylene carbonate are described by an empirical rate equation of the form previously applied by Davidson and Cole to their measurements for 1-propanol, propylene glycol, and glycerol.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq9zOgRxphfbLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgkW40yd4wVtw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaE38XlsVGitb0%253D&amp;md5=77440f67c9d377ce409df848b6320653</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref38"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">38</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit38" data-doi="10.1021/jp076869m"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Umebayashi, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mitsugi, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fukuda, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fujimori, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fujii, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kanzaki, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Takeuchi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ishiguro, S.-I.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Lithium Ion Solvation in Room-Temperature Ionic Liquids Involving Bis(trifluoromethanesulfonyl) Imide Anion Studied by Raman Spectroscopy and DFT Calculations</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit38R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">38</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Lithium ion solvation in room-temperature ionic liquids involving bis(trifluoromethanesulfonyl) imide anion studied by Raman spectroscopy and DFT calculations</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Umebayashi, Yasuhiro; Mitsugi, Takushi; Fukuda, Shuhei; Fujimori, Takao; Fujii, Kenta; Kanzaki, Ryo; Takeuchi, Munetaka; Ishiguro, Shin-Ichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry B</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The solvation structure of the lithium ion in room-temp. ionic liqs. 1-ethyl-3-methylimidazolium bis(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide (EMI+TFSI-) and N-butyl-N-methylpyrrolidinium bis(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide (BMP+TFSI-) has been studied by Raman spectroscopy and DFT calcns.                         cas_cit39R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">39</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ohm's law for ion conduction in lithium and beyond-lithium battery electrolytes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Galluzzo, Michael D.; Maslyn, Jacqueline A.; Shah, Deep B.; Balsara, Nitash P.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Chemical Physics</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">151</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">020901/1-020901/11</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JCPSA6</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Institute of Physics</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The viability of next generation lithium and beyond-lithium battery technologies hinges on the development of electrolytes with improved performance.  Comparing electrolytes is not straightforward as multiple electrochem. parameters affect the performance of an electrolyte.  Addnl. complications arise due to the formation of concn. gradients in response to dc potentials.  We propose a modified version of Ohm's law to analyze current through binary electrolytes driven by a small dc potential.  We show that the proportionality const. in Ohm's law is given by the product of the ionic cond., κ, and the ratio of currents in the presence (iss) and absence (iΩ) of concn. gradients, ρ+.  The importance of ρ+ was recognized by Evans et al. [Polymer 28, 2324 (1987)].  The product κρ+ is used to rank order a collection of electrolytes.  Ideally, both κ and ρ+ should be maximized, but we observe a trade-off between these two parameters, resulting in an upper bound.  This trade-off is analogous to the famous Robeson upper bound for permeability and selectivity in gas sepn. membranes.  Designing polymer electrolytes that overcome this trade-off is an ambitious but worthwhile goal.                         cas_cit40R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">40</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Onsager Transport Coefficients and Transference Numbers in Polyelectrolyte Solutions and Polymerized Ionic Liquids</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fong, Kara D.; Self, Julian; McCloskey, Bryan D.; Persson, Kristin A.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Macromolecules (Washington, DC, United States)</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Electrolytes featuring neg. charged polymers, such as nonaq. polyelectrolyte solns. and polymd. ionic liqs., are currently under investigation as potential high cation transference no. (t+) electrolytes for lithium-ion batteries.  Herein, we use coarse-grained mol. dynamics simulations to characterize the Onsager transport coeffs. of polyelectrolyte solns. as a function of chain length and concn.  For all the systems studied, we find that the rigorously computed transference no. is substantially lower than that approximated by the ideal soln. (Nernst-Einstein) equations typically used to characterize these systems due to the presence of strong anion-anion and cation-anion correlations.  None of the polyelectrolyte solns. achieve t+ greater than that of the conventional binary salt electrolyte, with some solns. having neg. t+.                         cas_cit41R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">41</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Stable and unstable diglyme-based electrolytes for batteries with sodium or graphite as electrode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Goktas, Mustafa; Bolli, Christoph; Buchheim, Johannes; Berg, Erik J.; Novak, Petr; Bonilla, Francisco; Rojo, Teofilo; Komaba, Shinichi; Kubota, Kei; Adelhelm, Philipp</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We study the stability of several diglyme-based electrolytes in sodium/sodium and sodium/graphite cells.  The electrolyte behavior for different conductive salts [sodium trifluoromethanesulfonate (NaOTf), NaPF6, NaClO4, bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide (NaFSI), and sodium bis(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide (NaTFSI)] is compared and, in some cases, considerable differences are identified.  Side reactions are studied with a variety of methods, including X-ray diffraction, SEM, transmission electron microscopy, online electrochem. mass spectrometry, and in situ electrochem. dilatometry.  For Na/Na sym. cells as well as for Na/graphite cells, we find that NaOTf and NaPF6 are the preferred salts followed by NaClO4 and NaFSI, as the latter two lead to more side reactions and increasing impedance.  NaTFSI shows the worst performance leading to poor Coulombic efficiency and cycle life.  In this case, excessive side reactions lead also to a strong increase in electrode thickness during cycling.  In a qual. order, the suitability of the conductive salts can be ranked as follows: NaOTf ≥ NaPF6 &gt; NaClO4 &gt; NaFSI » NaTFSI.  Our results also explain two recent, seemingly conflicting findings on the degree of solid electrolyte interphase formation on graphite electrodes in sodium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqt-3Bm3bTk7LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhlzq-Nd6J-7g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXhsFCqu73O&amp;md5=b7950ed1ed50ddeda2ce3f151f4cb901</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref42"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">42</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit42" data-doi="10.1007/bf01337937"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sauerbrey, G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Verwendung von Schwingquarzen zur Wägung dünner Schichten und zur Mikrowägung</span>. <i>Zeitschrift für Physik</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1959</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>155</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">206</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">222</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit42R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">42</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The use of quartz oscillators for weighing thin layers and for microweighing</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sauerbrey, Gunther</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Zeitschrift fuer Physik</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">The frequency of a quartz plate is altered if a layer of foreign material is deposited on the quartz, e.g. by evapn.  Since changes in frequency can be measured very accurately, the phenomenon can be used for weighing thin layers.  The change in frequency is proportional to the d. of the foreign layer and the proportionality const. can be calcd. from the characteristic frequency of the quartz plate.  The accuracy of the method is limited by the temp. variation of the characteristic frequency of the quartz oscillator.  For a temp. fluctuation of 1° the accuracy is ±4.10-9 g./cm.  This corresponds to a mean thickness of the layer of 0.4 A. at a d. of 1 g./cc.  The method can also be used for direct microweighing, e.g. by evapg. a drop of a soln. on the quartz plate.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrm8hLTeIGERbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhlzq-Nd6J-7g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaG1MXoslSjtQ%253D%253D&amp;md5=ff97a9cc63637eb9823aebfeb57a367b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit43" data-doi="10.1149/1.1357316"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Moshkovich, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gofer, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Aurbach, D.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Investigation of the Electrochemical Windows of Aprotic Alkali Metal (Li, Na, K) Salt Solutions</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit43R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">43</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Investigation of the electrochemical windows of aprotic alkali metal (Li, Na, K) salt solutions</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Moshkovich, M.; Gofer, Y.; Aurbach, D.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2001</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">148</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">This work is a comparative study of the electrochem. windows and the basic processes on gold electrodes in LiClO4, NaClO4, and KClO4 solns. in propylene carbonate (PC).  The anal. tools included cyclic voltammetry, electrochem. quartz crystal microbalance, surface-sensitive FTIR spectroscopy (ex situ, external reflectance mode), and XPS.  The apparent electrochem. windows of these systems are anodically limited at potentials &gt;1.3 V (vs. Ag pseudoreference electrode corresponding to 4.3 vs. Li/Li+) due to solvent oxidn.  The apparent cathodic side is limited due to the reversible bulk active metal deposition occurring at ∼-3 and &lt;-2.7 V vs. Ag pseudoreference electrode for Li and Na, resp.  In the case of the potassium salt soln., the electrochem. window is limited by a pronounced cathodic process &lt;-2 V (vs. Ag ref. electrode), which is attributed to irreversible redn. of soln. species.  Irreversible potassium deposition occurs at potentials &lt;-2.5 V.  This process cannot be sepd. from the redn. processes of the soln. starting &lt;-2 V.  The study revealed that irreversible trace O2, trace H2O, and PC redn. form passivating surface films on these electrodes.  These films act as a solid electrolyte interphase, i.e., they allow transport of the alkali metal ions through them.  The study also found that the major constituent in the surface films is the PC redn. product CH3CH(OCO2M)CH2OCO2M.  In general, the surface films formed on the noble metal electrodes in the Li and K salt solns. are more stable than those formed in the Na salt solns., because the sodium oxides, hydroxide, and carbonates thus formed are more sol. in PC than the corresponding Li and K compds.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqebu4LDV0LJ7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljjjJbYrEXJTg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD3MXivVahu78%253D&amp;md5=2750b22563f9271bdcd29abffa4119ac</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref44"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">44</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit44" data-doi="10.1149/2.0191915jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sonoki, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Matsui, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Imanishi, N.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Effect of Anion Species in Early Stage of SEI Formation Process</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We investigate the influences that functional group, polymer mol. wt. and polymer mol. architecture have on the adsorption behavior of some sulfur contg. oligo(ethylene glycol) polymers to gold.  QCM-D and XPS was used in this investigation revealing that disulfide functional groups bind with more mass deposited than dithio, trithio or thiols.  This was obsd. with small di(ethylene glycol) polymers and with higher mass polymers.  The effect of the sulfo-groups was not as apparent with higher mass polymers.                         cas_cit46R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">46</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Designing Artificial Solid-Electrolyte Interphases for Single-Ion and High-Efficiency Transport in Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Tu, Zhengyuan; Choudhury, Snehashis; Zachman, Michael J.; Wei, Shuya; Zhang, Kaihang; Kourkoutis, Lena F.; Archer, Lynden A.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Joule</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Substrates able to rectify transport of ions based on charge and/or size are ubiquitous in biol. systems.  Electrolytes and interphases that selectively transport electrochem. active ions are likewise of broad interest in all elec. energy storage technologies.  In lithium-ion batteries, electrolytes with single- or near-single-ion cond. reduce losses caused by ion polarization.  In emergent lithium or sodium metal batteries, they maintain high cond. at the anode and stabilize metal deposition by fundamental mechanisms.  We report that 20- to 300-nm-thick, single-ion-conducting membranes deposited at the anode enable electrolytes with the highest combination of cation transference no., ionic cond., and electrochem. stability reported.  By means of direct visualization we find that single-ion membranes also reduce dendritic deposition of Li in liqs.  Galvanostatic measurements further show that the electrolytes facilitate long (3 mAh) recharge of full Li/LiNi0.8Co0.15Al0.05O2 (NCA) cells with high cathode loadings (3 mAh cm-2/19.9 mg cm-2) and at high current densities (3 mA cm-2).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqUuG8bNxcIlbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljjjJbYrEXJTg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXpsl2kur8%253D&amp;md5=43c25a5c33a2d70123b35210b1b6a68b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref47"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">47</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit47" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2016.03.089"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Darwiche, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bodenes, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Madec, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Monconduit, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Martinez, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Impact of The Salts and Solvents on The SEI Formation in Sb/Na Batteries: An XPS Analysis</span>. <i>Electrochim.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A thorough XPS is carried out on the Sb/Na electrochem. cell to rationalize the different electrochem. performances in terms of irreversible capacity on 1st cycle, cumulated capacity losses, reversible capacity and capacity retention.  Surface anal. of Sb electrode cycled vs. Na using three different electrolyte formulations by changing the nature of (NaClO4 or NaPF6) and the nature of the solvents (PC or EC/DMC mixt.) was performed by a combined XPS core peaks and quantification data anal. to establish the main components of the solid electrolyte interphase film (SEI) and its evolution on cycling.  The passivation layer formed during cycling is mainly constituted of carbonates, Na2CO3 and alkylcarbonates, that appear during the discharge after the preferential deposition of NaF from the degrdn. of FEC.  The solvent appears to have a key role, and the NaClO4/PC electrolyte, despite the high viscosity of its solvent gives the best compromise in terms of performances by forming a stable and thick SEI at the surface of all components of the composite electrode.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrrDy_xYF3XIrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljjjJbYrEXJTg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XnvVart7k%253D&amp;md5=bc99b35e2faf1c6086a54ed5e743549b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref48"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">48</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit48" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.7b15117"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zheng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Engelhard, M.                         cas_cit48R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">48</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Effects of Imide-Orthoborate Dual-Salt Mixtures in Organic Carbonate Electrolytes on the Stability of Lithium Metal Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Xing; Zheng, Jianming; Engelhard, Mark H.; Mei, Donghai; Li, Qiuyan; Jiao, Shuhong; Liu, Ning; Zhao, Wengao; Zhang, Ji-Guang; Xu, Wu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The effects of lithium imide and lithium orthoborate dual-salt electrolytes of different salt chemistries in carbonate solvents on the cycling stability of lithium (Li) metal batteries are systematically and comparatively investigated.  Two imide salts (LiTFSI and LiFSI) and two orthoborate salts (LiBOB and LiDFOB) are chosen for this study and compared with the conventional LiPF6 salt.  D. functional theory calcns. indicate that the chem. and electrochem. stabilities rank in the following order: LiTFSI-LiBOB &gt; LiTFSI-LiDFOB &gt; LiFSI-LiDFOB &gt; LiFSI-LiBOB.  The exptl. cycling stability of the Li metal batteries with the electrolytes ranks in the following order: LiTFSI-LiBOB &gt; LiTFSI-LiDFOB &gt; LiFSI-LiDFOB &gt; LiPF6 &gt; LiFSI-LiBOB, which is in well accordance with the calcn. results.  The LiTFSI-LiBOB can effectively protect the Al substrate and form a more robust surface film on Li metal anode, while the LiFSI-LiBOB results in serious corrosion to the stainless steel cell case and a thicker and looser surface film on Li anode.  The key findings of this work emphasize that the salt chem. is critically important for enhancing the interfacial stability of Li metal anode and should be carefully manipulated in the development of high-performance Li metal batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo3wJrRO8WibrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgHZrUYCKvong"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXntlWqtg%253D%253D&amp;md5=84fbc52bd70ea559d021f2c0a97378ad</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref49"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">49</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit49" data-doi="10.5796/electrochemistry.19-00073"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Morikawa, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yamada, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Doi, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nishimura, S.-i.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yamada, A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Reversible and High-rate Hard Carbon Negative Electrodes in a Fluorine-free Sodium-salt Electrolyte</span>. <i>Electrochemistry</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>88</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">151</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">156</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit49R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">49</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Reversible and high-rate hard carbon negative electrodes in a fluorine-free sodium-salt electrolyte</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Morikawa, Yusuke; Yamada, Yuki; Doi, Kyosuke; Nishimura, Shin-ichi; Yamada, Atsuo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochemistry (Tokyo, Japan)</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbon is widely studied as a promising neg. electrode in sodium-ion batteries.  To achieve its stable charge-discharge reaction, a fluorine-rich passivation film arising from a fluorinated salt or solvent in an electrolyte was demonstrated to be effective, but its essential role remained unclear.  Here, we report a sodium tetraphenylborate (NaBPh4)/1,2-dimethoxyethane (DME) electrolyte that is free from fluorine but enables the highly stable and high-rate charge-discharge cycling of hard carbon electrodes as compared to other combinations of Na salts and solvents. Hence, fluorine-based components are not indispensable to stabilize the hard carbon/electrolyte interface.         </div><div class="casAbstract">High sp. surface area carbon (HSSAC) is a class of promising high-capacity anode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  A crit. bottleneck of the HSSAC anode, however, is the ultra-low initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) in commonly used ester-based electrolytes.  This phenomenon further prohibits improving the specific capacity, long-term stability and rate capability of HSSAC anodes.  This work reports the largely enhanced anode performance of several different HSSAC anodes in ether-based electrolytes.  Very importantly, with the reduced graphene oxide (rGO) anode as one example, the ICE can be as high as 74.6% accompanied by a large reversible specific capacity of 509 mA h g-1 after 100 cycles at a c.d. of 0.1 A g-1. 75.2% of the capacity was retained after 1000 cycles at 1 A g-1.  Even at a high c.d. of 5 A g-1, the specific capacity of the rGO anode can be obtained at 196 mA h g-1.  This extraordinary performance is ascribed to the stable, thin, compact, uniform and ion conducting solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) formed in an ether-based electrolyte.  Fortunately, this SEI-modifying strategy is generic and is independent of the specific microstructures of HSSAC anodes, indicating a promising avenue for manipulating the SEI on HSSAC anodes through utilizing ether solvents to enable achievement of high ICE for large-capacity HSSAC anodes for practical applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq2QXpPrjk4ZbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgHZrUYCKvong"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XitV2mtbnN&amp;md5=be5958dc01f38deeacf6a2a0eb32101f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref51"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">51</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit51" data-doi="10.1016/s0378-7753(00)00431-6"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Aurbach, D.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Review of Selected Electrode–Solution Interactions which Determine the Performance of Li and Li Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit51R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">51</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Review of selected electrode-solution interactions which determine the performance of Li and Li ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Aurbach, D.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review with 67 refs. on several phenomenol. electrode-soln. interactions which det. the performance of lithium and lithium ion batteries.  This review is based on extensive studies of the behavior of Li, lithiated carbons and lithiated transition metal oxide electrodes in a wide variety of non-aq. electrolyte solns.  These studies included spectroscopic measurements (FTIR, XPS, EDAX), morphol. and structural anal. (XRD, SEM, AFM) in conjunction with impedance spectroscopy, EQCM and std. electrochem. techniques.  It appears that the performance of both Li, Li-C anodes and LixMOy cathodes depends on their surface chem. in solns.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) between Li and ionic liq. electrolytes was investigated in 1-methyl-1-propylpyrrolidinium bis(fluorosulfonyl)amide (MPPFSA) and 1-methyl-1-methoxyethylpyrrolidinium bis(fluorosulfonyl)amide (MOEMPFSA) with different LiFSA concns.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Understanding (electro-)chem. reactions at the electrode-electrolyte interface (EEI) is crucial to promote the cycle life of lithium-ion batteries.  In this study, we developed an in situ Fourier-transform IR spectroscopy (FT-IR) method, which provided unprecedented information on the oxidn. of carbonate solvents via dehydrogenation on LiNixMnyCo1-x-yO2 (NMC).                         cas_cit56R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">56</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Theoretical insights into the reductive decompositions of propylene carbonate and vinylene carbonate: density functional theory studies</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Yixuan; Balbuena, Perla B.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry B</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2002</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">106</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">17</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electro-reductive decompns. of propylene carbonate (PC) and vinylene carbonate (VC) in lithium-ion battery-electrolyte solns. have been investigated with d. functional theory using Li+(PC)n (n = 2, 3) and (PC)nLi+(VC) (n = 1, 2) cluster models.  The objective is to help in understanding the exptl. obsd. irreversible capacity of graphite anodes when PC alone is used as a solvent vs. the reported reversible capacity found in the presence of small amts. of VC in PC-based electrolyte solns.  PC solvates Li+ more strongly than ethylene carbonate (EC) and VC do, which implies that, if it exists, the co-intercalation of PC with Li ion into graphite layers is preferred; however, PC is more difficult to be reduced than the other two carbonates.  On the other hand, the reaction kinetics for the reductive decompn. of PC is very similar to that of EC.  Besides the problem caused by the possible co-intercalation, the nature of the PC redn. products may also be responsible for its disability to passivate the graphite surface.  The theor. results suggest the following explanations for the role of VC as an additive: A solvated VC in the (PC)nLi+(VC) complex is initially reduced to a more stable ion-pair intermediate, which will undergo a ring-opening reaction by a homolytic C (carbonyl carbon)-O rupture in two ways.  One is that the reduced VC decomps. to form a radical anion via a barrier of about 20 kcal/mol and the subsequent radical anion termination would generate the proper products, dominated by unsatd. lithium alkyl dicarbonates, such as lithium vinylene dicarbonate (CHOCO2Li)2 and lithium divinylene dicarbonate (CH:CHOCO2Li)2, to build up an effective solid electrolyte interfacial (SEI) film.  The unsatd. lithium dicarbonates may be further polymd., forming lithium polyvinylene dicarbonate or oligomers with several repeated CH:CH units that would considerably improve the passivation of graphite electrode in the presence of VC because of formation of a more cohesive and flexible film.  The alternative way is that, starting from the VC-redn. intermediate, a ring opening occurs on the unreduced PC moiety rather than on the reduced VC, via a lower barrier.  Briefly, VC plays an important role in terms of thermodn. factors by somehow weakening the PC co-intercalation into graphite, stabilizing the redn. ion-pair intermediate, and generating more proper film-forming agents.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqi0-zZMkPC27Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0likh6IQa-WGJA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD38XisVeksL4%253D&amp;md5=36a31a051428bb74fcbeb373b5f9fe5b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref57"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">57</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit57" data-doi="10.1149/1.1633765"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Vollmer, J.                         cas_cit60R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">60</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Density Functional Theory for Planar Electric Double Layers: Closing the Gap between Simple and Polyelectrolytes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Zhidong; Wu, Jianzhong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry B</span>
