        </div><div class="casAbstract">We report a nonlocal d. functional theory (NLDFT) for polyelectrolyte solns. within the primitive model; i.e., the solvent is represented by a continuous dielec. medium, and the small ions and polyions by single and tangentially connected charged hard spheres, resp.  The excess Helmholtz energy functional is derived from a modified fundamental measure theory for hard-sphere repulsion, an extended first-order thermodn. perturbation theory for chain connectivity, and a quadratic functional Taylor expansion for electrostatic correlations.  With the direct and cavity correlation functions of the corresponding monomeric systems as inputs, the NLDFT predicts the segment-level microscopic structures and adsorption isotherms of polyelectrolytes at oppositely charged surfaces in good agreement with mol. simulations.  In particular, it faithfully reproduces the layering structures of polyions, charge inversion, and overcharging that cannot be captured by alternative methods including the polyelectrolyte Poisson-Boltzmann equation and an earlier version of DFT.  The NLDFT has also been used to investigate the influences of the small ion valence, polyion chain length, and size disparity between polyion segments and counterions on the microscopic structure, mean electrostatic potential, and overcharging in planar elec. double layers contg. polyelectrolytes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqAtmpm5y_in7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljfG71H5a8A0g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD28XisFWju7o%253D&amp;md5=658b1a3f33db11fe12d37ffdbf843f8b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref61"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref61'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">61</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit61" data-doi="10.1021/jacs.7b09156"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Matsumoto, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shimizu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sotoike, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Watanabe, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Iwasa, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Itoh, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Aida, T.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Exceptionally High Electric Double Layer Capacitances of Oligomeric Ionic Liquids</span>. <i>J. However, only few electrolytes can simultaneously fulfill these two requisites.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The polymeric cations of polymeric ionic liqs. (PILs) can adsorb from the bulk of a conventional ionic liq. (IL) to the Au(1 1 1) electrode interface and form a boundary layer.  The interfacial properties of the PIL boundary layer may be tuned by potential.  Atomic force microscopy has been used to investigate the changes of surface morphol., normal and lateral forces of a 5 wt% PIL/IL mixt. as a function of potential.  Polymeric cations adsorb strongly to Au(1 1 1) and form a polymeric cation-enriched boundary layer at -1.0 V.  This boundary layer binds less strongly to the surface at open circuit potential (OCP) and weakly at + 1.0 V.  The polymeric cation chains are compressed at -1.0 V and OCP owing to elec. attractions with the electrode surface, but fully stretched at + 1.0 V due to elec. repulsions.  The lateral forces of the 5 wt% PIL/IL mixt. at -1.0 V and OCP are higher than at + 1.0 V as the polymeric cation-enriched boundary layer is rougher and has stronger interactions with the AFM probe; at + 1.0 V, the lateral force is low and comparable to pure conventional IL due to displacement of polymeric cations with conventional anions in the boundary layer.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq5yn1heX6cjrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg_6Iv2BXzvig"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3MXhvFGjtbjM&amp;md5=ae3bc189f3cc9c3b900649728578966f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref63"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref63'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">63</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit63" data-doi="10.1016/j.snb.2006.03.024"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sauvage, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Baudrin, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tarascon, J. Na intercalation in battery system has been reported since the early 1980s but Na-ion batteries are not commercialized so far though studies on Li-ion batteries have been reported since the late 1970s and the practical batteries have been extensively utilized for portable device applications in the world since 1991.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The physicochem. properties of pentaglyme (G5) and sodium bis(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)amide (Na[TFSA]) binary mixts. were investigated with respect to salt concn. and temp.  The d., viscosity, ionic cond., self-diffusion coeff., and oxidative stability of a series of binary mixts. were measured, and the mixts. were examd. as electrolytes for Na secondary batteries.  An equimolar mixt. of G5 and Na[TFSA] formed a low melting solvate, [Na(G5)1][TFSA], which exhibited an ionic cond. of 0.61 mS cm-1 at 30°.  The ionicity (Λimp/Λideal) of the glyme-Na[TFSA] mixt. was estd. from the molar cond. of electrochem. impedance measurements (Λimp) and the Walden plot (Λideal).  [Na(G5)1][TFSA] possessed a high ionicity of 0.63 at 30°, suggesting that [Na(G5)1][TFSA] is highly dissocd. into a [Na(G5)1]+ cation and a [TFSA]- anion, regardless of the extreme salt concn. in the liq.  The oxidative stabilities of G5-Na[TFSA] mixts. were investigated by linear sweep voltammetry, and the higher concn. resulted in higher oxidative stability due to the lowering of the HOMO energy level of G5 by complexation with the Na+ ion.  In addn., battery tests were performed using the mixts. as electrolytes.  The [Na|[Na(G5)1][TFSA]|Na0.44MnO2] cell showed good charge-discharge cycle stability, with a discharge capacity of ca. 100 mA h g-1, while the [Na(G5)1.25][TFSA] system, contg. excess G5, showed poor stability.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrciKkdDgBQh7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0libKu58Vd0qZA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXotlyqsb8%253D&amp;md5=9822987fef5ac09109afe95cfa247c47</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref68"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref68'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">68</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit68" data-doi="10.1149/2.0811605jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wohde, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Balabajew, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Roling, B.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Li+ Transference Numbers in Liquid Electrolytes Obtained by Very-Low-Frequency Impedance Spectroscopy at Variable Electrode Distances</span>. <i>J. Electrochem. Soc.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>163</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">A714</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1149/2.0811605jes</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D163%26publication_year%3D2016%26pages%3DA714%26journal%3DJ.%2BElectrochem.%2BSoc.%26author%3DF.%2BWohde%26author%3DM.%2BBalabajew%26author%3DB.%2BRoling%26title%3DLi%252B%2BTransference%2BNumbers%2Bin%2BLiquid%2BElectrolytes%2BObtained%2Bby%2BVery-Low-Frequency%2BImpedance%2BSpectroscopy%2Bat%2BVariable%2BElectrode%2BDistances%26doi%3D10.1149%252F2.0811605jes&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c21557&amp;doiOfLink=10.1149%2F2.0811605jes&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit68R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">68</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Li+ Transference Numbers in Liquid Electrolytes Obtained by Very-Low-Frequency Impedance Spectroscopy at Variable Electrode Distances</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wohde, F.; Balabajew, M.; Roling, B.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">163</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">A714-A721</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JESOAN</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4651</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The Li+ transference nos. of three different liq. electrolytes for Li-ion batteries were measured in a sym. Li/electrolyte/Li cell by very-low-frequency impedance spectroscopy (VLF-IS).  The electrolytes were: (i) The std. battery electrolyte LP30; (ii) an equimolar mixt. of tetraglyme (G4) and Li bis(trifluoromethylsulfonyl)imide (Li-TFSI); (iii) Li-TFSI dissolved in the ionic liq. 1-butyl-1-methyl-pyrrolidinium bis(trifluoromethylsulfonyl)imide (BMP-TFSI).  The Li+ transference nos. of the two electrolytes LP30 and G4/Li-TFSI are much smaller than the Li+ transport nos. [Formula] obtained from pulsed-field gradient NMR measurements.  However, in the case of BMP-TFSI/Li-TFSI, the values for [Formula] and [Formula] are more similar.  To rationalize the large differences between [Formula] and [Formula] found for LP30 and G4/Li-TFSI, the authors combined the Onsager reciprocal relations with linear response theory, and the authors derived expressions for [Formula], which take into account all correlations between ionic movements in the electrolyte.  Thereby, [Formula] can be considerably smaller than [Formula], if strong directional correlations exist between the movements of cations and anions.  Finally, differences in Li+ transference nos. obtained by VLF-IS and by potentiostatic polarization measurements are discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoSzXPGV9JxYbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhvS4Voxyp61g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XjtFKmtrk%253D&amp;md5=8be182b8bdb62df588b88c91119fc9f7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref69"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref69'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">69</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit69"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kondou, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dokko, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Watanabe, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ueno, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Rate Performance of LiCoO2 Half-cells Using Highly Concentrated Lithium Bis(fluorosulfonyl)amide Electrolytes and Their Relevance to Transport Properties</span>. <i>Electrochemistry</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2021</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>21</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">00052</span></span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D21%26publication_year%3D2021%26pages%3D00052%26journal%3DElectrochemistry%26author%3DS.%2BKondou%26author%3DK.%2BDokko%26author%3DM.%2BWatanabe%26author%3DK.%2BUeno%26title%3DRate%2BPerformance%2Bof%2BLiCoO2%2BHalf-cells%2BUsing%2BHighly%2BConcentrated%2BLithium%2BBis%2528fluorosulfonyl%2529amide%2BElectrolytes%2Band%2BTheir%2BRelevance%2Bto%2BTransport%2BProperties&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c21557&amp;doiOfLink=&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            cas_R

                10.1039/c6cs00776g</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGot-2IKXj-Q-7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liDanmQ8T4p2A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXkvF2rsbk%253D&amp;md5=cfc7b2d153696dcbded22d954ff06174</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1021/cr500192f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahbi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Research Development on Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review of sodium-ion battery technol.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-metal batteries with conventional org. liq. electrolytes have disadvantages including dendrite deposition and safety concern.  In this work, we report a low-flammable electrolyte (NaPF6-FRE) consisting of 1 M NaPF6 in 1,2-dimethoxyethane (DME), fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC), and 1,1,1,3,3,3-hexafluoroisopropylmethyl ether (HFPM) (2:1:2, in vol. ratio).  The sym. Na and Na||Cu cells with a 1 M NaPF6-DME electrolyte absorbed in a porous separator, such as the porous glass-fiber, show very poor cycling performance.  In addn., the cell with a Na3V2(PO4)3 (NVP) cathode and 1 M NaPF6-DME electrolyte shows low Coulombic efficiency.  FEC was added into the NaPF6-DME-based electrolyte to reduce the irreversible capacity of the NVP cathode and improve the Coulombic efficiency of the cell.  However, the high reactivity of FEC with the Na electrode leads to formation of an unstable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) and large interfacial resistance, and HFPM was further added to stabilize the Na electrode surface by forming a new fluorine-contg. org. layer.  The new prepd. low-flammable electrolyte (NaPF6-FRE) with 1 M NaPF6 in DME, FEC, and HFPM (2:1:2, in vol. ratio) shows a wide electrochem. window of 5.2 V.  The Na sym. cells with this low-flammable electrolyte show superior cycling performance for 800 h with a stable voltage profile at 0.5 mA cm-2, 0.5 mA h cm-2 and 1 mA cm-2, 1 mA h cm-2, resp.  The NVP||Na cells show an excellent capacity retention of 94% after 2000 cycles and superior Coulombic efficiency of 99.9% on av. at 5 C.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp0GpDKd0RIw7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liDanmQ8T4p2A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXitFOms73I&amp;md5=8cd07959d04c27ed9b60b6af1ad44e1f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.1021/acsaem.0c02510"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High-Performance Sodium Metal Batteries with Sodium–Bismuth Alloy Anode</span>. <i>ACS Applied Energy Materials</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>3</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">12607</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">12612</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">For the application of sodium batteries, one challenge is the irregular deposition and dendrite growth during the cycling process.  Herein, it is reported that spontaneous alloying reactions can occur by the mech. mixing and pressing processes of the sodium metal and semimetal bismuth powder, which can regulate the deposition of sodium ions.  The improvement of the surface tension and affinity to sodium ions of the metal electrode can inhibit the dendrite growth.  The alloy anode can be used not only in carbonate-based electrolytes but also in the flame-retardant electrolyte.  It is demonstrated that the Na-Bi alloy electrode in a carbonate electrolyte keeps stable cyclability with a much lower overpotential of 20 mV for more than 1200 h.  The voltage hysteresis can be effectively reduced by using the Na-Bi alloy anode.  The Na3V2(PO4)3 electrode with a flame-retardant electrolyte shows a Coulombic efficiency of 99.9% at 585 mA g-1 during long cycles.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The 1st review of the various electrolytes currently used and developed for Na-ion batteries (SIBs), both in terms of materials and concepts, is presented.  In contrast to the Li-ion battery (LIB), which is a mature technol. for which a more or less unanimously accepted std. electrolyte exists: 1 M LiPF6 in EC/DMC, the electrolyte of choice for SIBs has not yet fully conformed to a std.  This is true for both materials: salts, solvents, or additives, and concept, using the main track of org. solvents or aiming for other concepts.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbons (HC) as an anode material in sodium ion batteries, present enhanced electrochem. performances in ether-based electrolytes, making them promising potential in practical applications.  However, the underlying mechanism behind the excellent performances is still in question.  Here, ex-situ NMR, gas chromatog.-mass spectrometry and high-resoln. transmission electron microscope were used to clarify the insightful chem. of ether- and ester-based electrolytes in terms of solid-electrolyte-interphase (SEI) on hard carbons.  The results confirm the marked electrolyte decompn. and the formation of a SEI film in EC/DEC, but no SEI film in the case of diglyme.         </div><div class="casAbstract">It is often stated that formation of a functional solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) in sodium ion batteries is hampered by the higher soly. of SEI components such as sodium salts in comparison to the lithium analogs.  In order to investigate these phenomena, SEI formation and functionality, as well as cell self-discharge, are studied for the sodium ion system with comparative expts. on the equiv. lithium ion system.  By conducting a set of expts. on carbonaceous anodes, the impact of SEI dissoln. is tested.  The results show that the SEI layer in sodium ion cells is inferior to that in lithium ion counterparts with regards to self-discharge; sodium cells show a loss in capacity at a dramatic rate as compared to the lithium counterparts when they are stored at sodiated and lithiated states, resp., for a long time with no external applied current or potential.  Also, synchrotron-based hard XPS measurements indicate that the major factor leading to increased self-discharge is dissoln. of significant parts of the sodium-based SEI.  Furthermore, the effect of fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) electrolyte additive on self-discharge is tested as part of the work.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGokiL-DGoBWJbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liiksxMJ2JfZw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhvVWnu7nN&amp;md5=efb6371771df6ea383fed6449e8e5e1e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref9" class="rightTabRefNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit9" data-doi="10.1021/acscentsci.5b00328"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Seh, Z.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Owing to its low cost and high natural abundance, sodium metal is among the most promising anode materials for energy storage technologies beyond lithium ion batteries.  However, room-temp. sodium metal anodes suffer from poor reversibility during long-term plating and stripping, mainly due to formation of nonuniform solid electrolyte interphase as well as dendritic growth of sodium metal.  Herein it is reported for the first time that a simple liq. electrolyte, sodium hexafluorophosphate in glymes (mono-, di-, and tetraglyme), can enable highly reversible and nondendritic plating-stripping of sodium metal anodes at room temp.  High av. Coulombic efficiencies of 99.9% were achieved over 300 plating-stripping cycles at 0.5 mA cm-2.  The long-term reversibility was found to arise from the formation of a uniform, inorg. solid electrolyte interphase made of sodium oxide and sodium fluoride, which is highly impermeable to electrolyte solvent and conducive to nondendritic growth.  As a proof of concept, a room-temp. sodium-sulfur battery is demonstrated using this class of electrolytes, paving the way for the development of next-generation, sodium-based energy storage technologies.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrdqXOWzEW74bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liiksxMJ2JfZw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhvVGmtrfL&amp;md5=6155e3c781a24b8340081c2ad4444616</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref10" class="rightTabRefNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit10" data-doi="10.1002/inf2.12023"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lin, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chou, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Recent Research Progresses in Ether- and Ester-Based Electrolytes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>InfoMat</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>1</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">376</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">389</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

Here, hard carbons with a tailored bimodal pore network of internal micropores interconnected through mesopores are studied as sodium-ion battery anodes.  The evolution of their solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) is analyzed in three different electrolytes (NaPF6 in an ether-based solvent, and NaPF6 or NaClO4 in a carbonate-based system). While the presence of a fluorine-contg. salt appears to have a detg. role in the SEI stability, the electrochem. decompn. of carbonate-based solvents is detrimental for the long-term stability as the interfacial resistance increases. The continued search for routes to improve the power and energy d. of lithium ion batteries for elec. vehicles and consumer electronics has resulted in significant innovation in all cell components, particularly in electrode materials design.  In this Review, we highlight an often less noted route to improving energy d.: increasing the Li+ transference no. of the electrolyte.  Turning to Newman's original lithium ion battery models, we demonstrate that electrolytes with modestly higher Li+ transference nos. compared to traditional carbonate-based liq. electrolytes would allow higher power densities and enable faster charging (e.g., &gt;2C), even if their cond. was substantially lower than that of conventional electrolytes.                         rightTab-cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Poly(ethylene oxide) as a alternative host materials for Li+-conducting solid polymer electrolytes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Mindemark, Jonas; Lacey, Matthew J.; Bowden, Tim; Brandell, Daniel</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Progress in Polymer Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The bulk of the scientific literature on Li-conducting solid (solvent-free) polymer electrolytes (SPEs) for applications such as Li-based batteries is focused on polyether-based materials, not least the archetypal poly(ethylene oxide) (PEO).  A significant no. of alternative polymer hosts have, however, been explored over the years, encompassing materials such as polycarbonates, polyesters, polynitriles, polyalcs. and polyamines.  These display fundamentally different properties to those of polyethers, and might therefore be able to resolve the key issues restricting SPEs from realizing their full potential, for example in terms of ionic cond., chem. or electrochem. stability and temp. sensitivity.  It is further interesting that many of these polymer materials complex Li ions less strongly than PEO and facilitate ion transport through different mechanisms than polyethers, which is likely crit. for true advancement in the area.  In this review, &gt;30 years of research on these 'alternative' Li-ion-conducting SPE host materials are summarized and discussed in the perspective of their potential application in electrochem. devices, with a clear focus on Li batteries.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Connecting continuum-scale ion transport properties such as cond. and cation transference no. to microscopic transport properties such as ion dissocn. and ion self-diffusivities is an unresolved challenge in characterizing polymer electrolytes.  Better understanding of the relationship between microscopic and continuum scale transport properties would enable the rational design of improved electrolytes for applications such as lithium batteries.  We present measurements of continuum and microscopic ion transport properties of nonflammable liq. electrolytes consisting of binary mixts. of lithium bis(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide (LiTFSI) and perfluoropolyethers (PFPE) with different end groups: diol, di-Me carbonate, ethoxy-diol, and ethoxy-dimethyl carbonate.  The continuum properties, cond. and cation transference no., were measured by ac impedance spectroscopy and potentiostatic polarization, resp.  The ion self-diffusivities were measured by pulsed field gradient NMR spectroscopy (PFG-NMR), and a microscopic cation transference no. was calcd. from these measurements.  The measured ion self-diffusivities did not reflect the measured conductivities; in some cases, samples with high diffusivities exhibited low cond.  We introduce a nondimensional parameter, β, that combines microscopic diffusivities and cond.  We show that β is a sensitive function of end-group chem.  In the ethoxylated electrolytes, β is close to unity, the value expected for electrolytes that obey the Nernst-Einstein equation.  In these cases, the microscopic and continuum transference nos. are in reasonable agreement.  PFPE electrolytes devoid of ethoxy groups exhibit values of β that are significantly lower than unity.  In these cases, there is significant deviation between microscopic and continuum transference nos.  We propose that this may be due to electrostatic coupling of the cation and anion or contributions to the NMR signal from neutral ion pairs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpxUzpbtJYXW7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgeRNcT5bA2pw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XmvFGjsrg%253D&amp;md5=066353b09750183978dee493395f0bfc</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref20" class="rightTabRefNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit20" data-doi="10.1039/c7sm00794a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Timachova, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chintapalli, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Olson, K.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Perfluoropolyethers (PFPEs) are polymer electrolytes with fluorinated carbon backbones that have high flash points and have been shown to exhibit moderate conductivities and high cation transference nos. when mixed with lithium salts.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Polymer electrolytes provide a safe soln. for future solid-state high-energy-d. batteries. Herein, we demonstrate the versatility of a polymeric ionic liq. (PolyIL) as a polymer solvent to achieve this goal for both Na and K. However, the transport properties and Li-ion solvation in these media are not fully understood.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The widely known Stejskal-Tanner (ST) equation is central to any diffusion NMR or so-called pulsed gradient spin-echo expt., describing the signal attenuation due to loss of coherence caused by diffusion as a function of the exptl. parameters.  What is less widely known is that the equation itself is not invariable when applying different pulse sequences or different gradient pulse shapes and should thus be modified accordingly.  This concept is not new, but nevertheless experimentalists applying diffusion NMR in their research often overlook or are completely oblivious to this fact.  In this article, the derivation of the ST equation through the Bloch-Torrey equations is discussed in detail, followed by a discussion of the most basic NMR expts. that measure free diffusion.  This derivation is performed here in a novel way, leading to expressions for the ST equation that do not assume any gradient shape beforehand, leaving only a few parameters that are detd. solely by the gradient pulse shape.  This new approach to presenting the ST equation increases awareness of its dependence on gradient shape.  Moreover, it relieves designers of future diffusion NMR pulse sequences of struggling with the cumbersome task of deriving and reporting the equation for each gradient shape sep.  A quick ref. table of the ST equation for the basic diffusion NMR pulse sequences for any gradient shape is given, as well as an overview for most other diffusion NMR pulse sequences described in the literature. © 2012 Wiley Periodicals, Inc.     </div><div class="casAbstract">cf. C. A. 34, 303.8.  A simple, reasonable modification of H.'s previous theoretical treatment of the viscosity of dil. solns. of long-chain mols. leads to the equation ηsp/c = (ηsp/c)c=0 (1 + k'ηsp) for the initial concn. dependence of the viscosity.  This is identical with an equation arrived at empirically by Schulz and Blaschke (C. A. 36, 17.9).  The const. k' is characteristic of a given solute-solvent system; it is the same for solns., in a given solvent, of different members of a polymer-homologous series, however.  At low concns. (such as assumed in the theoretical derivation), this relation is equiv. to ηsp/c = (ηsp/c)2c=0 + k'(ηsp/c)2c=o C.  Equations proposed by Baker (C. A. 7, 4069), by Fikentscher and Mark (C. A. 24, 1010), by de Jong, Kruyt and Lens (C. A. 27, 458) and by Martin all reduce to this same limiting equation, with k' different for different systems.  Equations proposed by Arrhenius (Medd. Vetenskapsakad. Nobelinst. 4, 13 (1916)), by Hess and Philippoff (C. A. 31, 4873.3) and by Bred´ee and de Booys (C.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The viscosity of several polyelectrolytes is measured in both salt free solns. and solns. in the high salt limit.  At low polymer concns., the zero shear rate viscosity decreases as much as 100-fold upon addn. of a monovalent salt, namely NaCl.  However, as polymer concn. increases, the viscosity difference between polymer in salt free and in monovalent salt soln. diminishes.  Further, the zero shear rate viscosity becomes independent of added monovalent salt at the crit. polyelectrolyte concn. cD.  Above cD, the addn. of monovalent salt increases the zero shear rate viscosity of the entangled polyelectrolyte solns.  The viscosity increase agrees with viscosity scaling theory for polyelectrolytes in the entangled regime.  Polyelectrolytes exhibiting an increase in viscosity above cD in the presence of monovalent salt include three natural anionic polyelectrolytes (xanthan, carrageenan, welan), one synthetic anionic polyelectrolyte (hydrolyzed polyacrylamide), and one natural cationic polyelectrolyte (chitosan).  Generally, these polyelectrolytes are relatively high mol. wt. (&gt;1 M Da), which makes cD exptl. accessible (e.g., cD = 0.2 wt% for xanthan).  The magnitude of the viscosity increase is as high as 300% for xanthan and nearly independent of monovalent salt concn. in the high salt limit.  The increase in viscosity in monovalent salt soln. and magnitude of cD appear to be heavily influenced by the mol. characteristics of the polymers such as monomer wt., mol. structure, and chain conformation.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrG-L6ZP-QODbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liiQt_zdVYfng"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXlvVWjtrg%253D&amp;md5=63b181e00b6948d8646d5dd27deded1a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref37"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref37" class="rightTabRefNumLink">37</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit37" data-doi="10.1021/j100664a019"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Payne, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Theodorou, I.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The viability of next generation lithium and beyond-lithium battery technologies hinges on the development of electrolytes with improved performance.  Comparing electrolytes is not straightforward as multiple electrochem. parameters affect the performance of an electrolyte.  Addnl. complications arise due to the formation of concn. gradients in response to dc potentials.  We propose a modified version of Ohm's law to analyze current through binary electrolytes driven by a small dc potential.  We show that the proportionality const. in Ohm's law is given by the product of the ionic cond., κ, and the ratio of currents in the presence (iss) and absence (iΩ) of concn. gradients, ρ+.  The importance of ρ+ was recognized by Evans et al. [Polymer 28, 2324 (1987)].  The product κρ+ is used to rank order a collection of electrolytes.  Ideally, both κ and ρ+ should be maximized, but we observe a trade-off between these two parameters, resulting in an upper bound.  This trade-off is analogous to the famous Robeson upper bound for permeability and selectivity in gas sepn. membranes.  Designing polymer electrolytes that overcome this trade-off is an ambitious but worthwhile goal.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We study the stability of several diglyme-based electrolytes in sodium/sodium and sodium/graphite cells.  The electrolyte behavior for different conductive salts [sodium trifluoromethanesulfonate (NaOTf), NaPF6, NaClO4, bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide (NaFSI), and sodium bis(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide (NaTFSI)] is compared and, in some cases, considerable differences are identified.  Side reactions are studied with a variety of methods, including X-ray diffraction, SEM, transmission electron microscopy, online electrochem. mass spectrometry, and in situ electrochem. dilatometry.  For Na/Na sym. cells as well as for Na/graphite cells, we find that NaOTf and NaPF6 are the preferred salts followed by NaClO4 and NaFSI, as the latter two lead to more side reactions and increasing impedance.  NaTFSI shows the worst performance leading to poor Coulombic efficiency and cycle life.  In this case, excessive side reactions lead also to a strong increase in electrode thickness during cycling.  In a qual. order, the suitability of the conductive salts can be ranked as follows: NaOTf ≥ NaPF6 &gt; NaClO4 &gt; NaFSI » NaTFSI.     </div><div class="casAbstract">The frequency of a quartz plate is altered if a layer of foreign material is deposited on the quartz, e.g. by evapn.  Since changes in frequency can be measured very accurately, the phenomenon can be used for weighing thin layers.  The change in frequency is proportional to the d. of the foreign layer and the proportionality const. can be calcd. from the characteristic frequency of the quartz plate.  The accuracy of the method is limited by the temp. variation of the characteristic frequency of the quartz oscillator.  For a temp. fluctuation of 1° the accuracy is ±4.10-9 g./cm.         </div><div class="casAbstract">This work is a comparative study of the electrochem. windows and the basic processes on gold electrodes in LiClO4, NaClO4, and KClO4 solns. in propylene carbonate (PC).  The anal. tools included cyclic voltammetry, electrochem. quartz crystal microbalance, surface-sensitive FTIR spectroscopy (ex situ, external reflectance mode), and XPS.  The apparent electrochem. windows of these systems are anodically limited at potentials &gt;1.3 V (vs. Ag pseudoreference electrode corresponding to 4.3 vs. Li/Li+) due to solvent oxidn.  The apparent cathodic side is limited due to the reversible bulk active metal deposition occurring at ∼-3 and &lt;-2.7 V vs. Ag pseudoreference electrode for Li and Na, resp.  In the case of the potassium salt soln., the electrochem. window is limited by a pronounced cathodic process &lt;-2 V (vs. Ag ref. electrode), which is attributed to irreversible redn. of soln. species.  Irreversible potassium deposition occurs at potentials &lt;-2.5 V.  This process cannot be sepd. from the redn. processes of the soln. starting &lt;-2 V.  The study revealed that irreversible trace O2, trace H2O, and PC redn. form passivating surface films on these electrodes.  These films act as a solid electrolyte interphase, i.e., they allow transport of the alkali metal ions through them.  The study also found that the major constituent in the surface films is the PC redn. product CH3CH(OCO2M)CH2OCO2M.  In general, the surface films formed on the noble metal electrodes in the Li and K salt solns. are more stable than those formed in the Na salt solns., because the sodium oxides, hydroxide, and carbonates thus formed are more sol. in PC than the corresponding Li and K compds.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqebu4LDV0LJ7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liXPruFp4V9fw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD3MXivVahu78%253D&amp;md5=2750b22563f9271bdcd29abffa4119ac</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref44"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref44" class="rightTabRefNumLink">44</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit44" data-doi="10.1149/2.0191915jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sonoki, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Matsui, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Imanishi, N.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Effect of Anion Species in Early Stage of SEI Formation Process</span>. <i>J. Electrolytes and interphases that selectively transport electrochem. active ions are likewise of broad interest in all elec. energy storage technologies.  In lithium-ion batteries, electrolytes with single- or near-single-ion cond. reduce losses caused by ion polarization.  In emergent lithium or sodium metal batteries, they maintain high cond. at the anode and stabilize metal deposition by fundamental mechanisms.  We report that 20- to 300-nm-thick, single-ion-conducting membranes deposited at the anode enable electrolytes with the highest combination of cation transference no., ionic cond., and electrochem. stability reported.  By means of direct visualization we find that single-ion membranes also reduce dendritic deposition of Li in liqs.  Galvanostatic measurements further show that the electrolytes facilitate long (3 mAh) recharge of full Li/LiNi0.8Co0.15Al0.05O2 (NCA) cells with high cathode loadings (3 mAh cm-2/19.9 mg cm-2) and at high current densities (3 mA cm-2).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqUuG8bNxcIlbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhtxgpYZ6jQaA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXpsl2kur8%253D&amp;md5=43c25a5c33a2d70123b35210b1b6a68b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref47"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref47" class="rightTabRefNumLink">47</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit47" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2016.03.089"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Darwiche, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bodenes, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Madec, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Monconduit, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Martinez, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Impact of The Salts and Solvents on The SEI Formation in Sb/Na Batteries: An XPS Analysis</span>. <i>Electrochim.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The effects of lithium imide and lithium orthoborate dual-salt electrolytes of different salt chemistries in carbonate solvents on the cycling stability of lithium (Li) metal batteries are systematically and comparatively investigated.  Two imide salts (LiTFSI and LiFSI) and two orthoborate salts (LiBOB and LiDFOB) are chosen for this study and compared with the conventional LiPF6 salt.  D. functional theory calcns. indicate that the chem. and electrochem. stabilities rank in the following order: LiTFSI-LiBOB &gt; LiTFSI-LiDFOB &gt; LiFSI-LiDFOB &gt; LiFSI-LiBOB. The LiTFSI-LiBOB can effectively protect the Al substrate and form a more robust surface film on Li metal anode, while the LiFSI-LiBOB results in serious corrosion to the stainless steel cell case and a thicker and looser surface film on Li anode.  The key findings of this work emphasize that the salt chem. is critically important for enhancing the interfacial stability of Li metal anode and should be carefully manipulated in the development of high-performance Li metal batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo3wJrRO8WibrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhtxgpYZ6jQaA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXntlWqtg%253D%253D&amp;md5=84fbc52bd70ea559d021f2c0a97378ad</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref49"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref49" class="rightTabRefNumLink">49</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit49" data-doi="10.5796/electrochemistry.19-00073"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Morikawa, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yamada, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Doi, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nishimura, S.-i.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yamada, A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Reversible and High-rate Hard Carbon Negative Electrodes in a Fluorine-free Sodium-salt Electrolyte</span>. <i>Electrochemistry</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>88</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">151</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">156</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

To achieve its stable charge-discharge reaction, a fluorine-rich passivation film arising from a fluorinated salt or solvent in an electrolyte was demonstrated to be effective, but its essential role remained unclear.  Here, we report a sodium tetraphenylborate (NaBPh4)/1,2-dimethoxyethane (DME) electrolyte that is free from fluorine but enables the highly stable and high-rate charge-discharge cycling of hard carbon electrodes as compared to other combinations of Na salts and solvents.  Surface anal. of the cycled electrode shows that the NaBPh4 is not decompd. during the cycle and that solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) is derived from DME.  Hence, fluorine-based components are not indispensable to stabilize the hard carbon/electrolyte interface.  The DME-derived SEI, though contg. no F component, can highly stabilize the interface to enable the reversible and high-rate cycling of hard carbon.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpkxezTfKcM7bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhtxgpYZ6jQaA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhtFCrsbjL&amp;md5=6566ef0dbcf80711ae67acb42f342f07</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref50"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref50" class="rightTabRefNumLink">50</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit50" data-doi="10.1039/c6ee03367a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, D.-W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lv, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zheng, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kang, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Q.-H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Achieving Superb Sodium Storage Performance on Carbon Anodes Through an Ether-Derived Solid Electrolyte Interphase</span>. <i>Energy Environ.         </div><div class="casAbstract">High sp. surface area carbon (HSSAC) is a class of promising high-capacity anode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  A crit. bottleneck of the HSSAC anode, however, is the ultra-low initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) in commonly used ester-based electrolytes.  This phenomenon further prohibits improving the specific capacity, long-term stability and rate capability of HSSAC anodes.  This work reports the largely enhanced anode performance of several different HSSAC anodes in ether-based electrolytes.  Very importantly, with the reduced graphene oxide (rGO) anode as one example, the ICE can be as high as 74.6% accompanied by a large reversible specific capacity of 509 mA h g-1 after 100 cycles at a c.d. of 0.1 A g-1. 75.2% of the capacity was retained after 1000 cycles at 1 A g-1.  Even at a high c.d. of 5 A g-1, the specific capacity of the rGO anode can be obtained at 196 mA h g-1.  This extraordinary performance is ascribed to the stable, thin, compact, uniform and ion conducting solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) formed in an ether-based electrolyte.  Fortunately, this SEI-modifying strategy is generic and is independent of the specific microstructures of HSSAC anodes, indicating a promising avenue for manipulating the SEI on HSSAC anodes through utilizing ether solvents to enable achievement of high ICE for large-capacity HSSAC anodes for practical applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq2QXpPrjk4ZbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhtxgpYZ6jQaA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XitV2mtbnN&amp;md5=be5958dc01f38deeacf6a2a0eb32101f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref51"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref51" class="rightTabRefNumLink">51</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit51" data-doi="10.1016/s0378-7753(00)00431-6"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Aurbach, D.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Review of Selected Electrode–Solution Interactions which Determine the Performance of Li and Li Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J. This review is based on extensive studies of the behavior of Li, lithiated carbons and lithiated transition metal oxide electrodes in a wide variety of non-aq. electrolyte solns.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Understanding (electro-)chem. reactions at the electrode-electrolyte interface (EEI) is crucial to promote the cycle life of lithium-ion batteries.  In this study, we developed an in situ Fourier-transform IR spectroscopy (FT-IR) method, which provided unprecedented information on the oxidn. of carbonate solvents via dehydrogenation on LiNixMnyCo1-x-yO2 (NMC).  While ethylene carbonate (EC) was stable against oxidn. on Pt up to 4.8 VLi, unique evidence for dehydrogenation of EC on LiNi0.8Co0.1Mn0.1O2 (NMC811) at voltages as low as 3.8 VLi was revealed by in situ FT-IR measurements, which was supported by d. functional theory (DFT) results.  Unique dehydrogenated species from EC were obsd. on NMC811 surface, including dehydrogenated EC anchored on oxides, vinylene carbonate (VC) and dehydrogenated oligomers which could diffuse away from the surface.  Similar dehydrogenation on NMC811 was noted for EMC-based and LP57 (1 M LiPF6 in 3 : 7 EC/EMC) electrolytes.  In contrast, no dehydrogenation was found for NMC111 or surface-modified NMC by coatings such as Al2O3.  In addn., while the dehydrogenation of solvents was obsd. in 1 M electrolytes with different anions, they were not obsd. on NMC811 in the concd. electrolyte (EC/EMC with 3.1 M LiPF6), indicating lithium coordination could suppress dehydrogenation.  Dehydrogenation of carbonates on NMC811 accompanied with rapid growth of interfacial impedance with increasing voltage revealed by electrochem. impedance spectroscopy (EIS), while the electrode-electrolyte combinations without dehydrogenation did not show significant impedance growth.  Therefore, minimizing carbonate dehydrogenation on the NMC surface by tuning electrode reactivity and electrolyte reactivity is crit. to develop high-energy Li-ion batteries with long cycle life.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqv06UGbz1wJrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li3KXeJIZpO3g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXitFOltrvF&amp;md5=4b617e0f8dcdb3daa1d02f2872a23b91</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref56"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref56" class="rightTabRefNumLink">56</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit56" data-doi="10.1021/jp014371t"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Balbuena, P.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The electro-reductive decompns. of propylene carbonate (PC) and vinylene carbonate (VC) in lithium-ion battery-electrolyte solns. have been investigated with d. functional theory using Li+(PC)n (n = 2, 3) and (PC)nLi+(VC) (n = 1, 2) cluster models.  The objective is to help in understanding the exptl. obsd. irreversible capacity of graphite anodes when PC alone is used as a solvent vs. the reported reversible capacity found in the presence of small amts. of VC in PC-based electrolyte solns.  PC solvates Li+ more strongly than ethylene carbonate (EC) and VC do, which implies that, if it exists, the co-intercalation of PC with Li ion into graphite layers is preferred; however, PC is more difficult to be reduced than the other two carbonates.  On the other hand, the reaction kinetics for the reductive decompn. of PC is very similar to that of EC.  Besides the problem caused by the possible co-intercalation, the nature of the PC redn. products may also be responsible for its disability to passivate the graphite surface.  The theor. results suggest the following explanations for the role of VC as an additive: A solvated VC in the (PC)nLi+(VC) complex is initially reduced to a more stable ion-pair intermediate, which will undergo a ring-opening reaction by a homolytic C (carbonyl carbon)-O rupture in two ways. The alternative way is that, starting from the VC-redn. intermediate, a ring opening occurs on the unreduced PC moiety rather than on the reduced VC, via a lower barrier.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We report a nonlocal d. functional theory (NLDFT) for polyelectrolyte solns. within the primitive model; i.e., the solvent is represented by a continuous dielec. medium, and the small ions and polyions by single and tangentially connected charged hard spheres, resp.  The excess Helmholtz energy functional is derived from a modified fundamental measure theory for hard-sphere repulsion, an extended first-order thermodn. perturbation theory for chain connectivity, and a quadratic functional Taylor expansion for electrostatic correlations.  With the direct and cavity correlation functions of the corresponding monomeric systems as inputs, the NLDFT predicts the segment-level microscopic structures and adsorption isotherms of polyelectrolytes at oppositely charged surfaces in good agreement with mol. simulations.  In particular, it faithfully reproduces the layering structures of polyions, charge inversion, and overcharging that cannot be captured by alternative methods including the polyelectrolyte Poisson-Boltzmann equation and an earlier version of DFT.  The NLDFT has also been used to investigate the influences of the small ion valence, polyion chain length, and size disparity between polyion segments and counterions on the microscopic structure, mean electrostatic potential, and overcharging in planar elec. double layers contg. polyelectrolytes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqAtmpm5y_in7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljyA85y-4gPWQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD28XisFWju7o%253D&amp;md5=658b1a3f33db11fe12d37ffdbf843f8b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref61"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref61" class="rightTabRefNumLink">61</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit61" data-doi="10.1021/jacs.7b09156"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Matsumoto, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shimizu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sotoike, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Watanabe, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Iwasa, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Itoh, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Aida, T.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Exceptionally High Electric Double Layer Capacitances of Oligomeric Ionic Liquids</span>. <i>J. (IL) to the Au(1 1 1) electrode interface and form a boundary layer.  The interfacial properties of the PIL boundary layer may be tuned by potential.  Atomic force microscopy has been used to investigate the changes of surface morphol., normal and lateral forces of a 5 wt% PIL/IL mixt. as a function of potential.  Polymeric cations adsorb strongly to Au(1 1 1) and form a polymeric cation-enriched boundary layer at -1.0 V.  This boundary layer binds less strongly to the surface at open circuit potential (OCP) and weakly at + 1.0 V.  The polymeric cation chains are compressed at -1.0 V and OCP owing to elec. attractions with the electrode surface, but fully stretched at + 1.0 V due to elec. repulsions.  The lateral forces of the 5 wt% PIL/IL mixt. at -1.0 V and OCP are higher than at + 1.0 V as the polymeric cation-enriched boundary layer is rougher and has stronger interactions with the AFM probe; at + 1.0 V, the lateral force is low and comparable to pure conventional IL due to displacement of polymeric cations with conventional anions in the boundary layer.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq5yn1heX6cjrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljyA85y-4gPWQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3MXhvFGjtbjM&amp;md5=ae3bc189f3cc9c3b900649728578966f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref63"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref63" class="rightTabRefNumLink">63</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit63" data-doi="10.1016/j.snb.2006.03.024"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sauvage, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Baudrin, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tarascon, J. The electrolytes were: (i) The std. battery electrolyte LP30; (ii) an equimolar mixt. of tetraglyme (G4) and Li bis(trifluoromethylsulfonyl)imide (Li-TFSI); (iii) Li-TFSI dissolved in the ionic liq. 1-butyl-1-methyl-pyrrolidinium bis(trifluoromethylsulfonyl)imide (BMP-TFSI). However, in the case of BMP-TFSI/Li-TFSI, the values for [Formula] and [Formula] are more similar.  To rationalize the large differences between [Formula] and [Formula] found for LP30 and G4/Li-TFSI, the authors combined the Onsager reciprocal relations with linear response theory, and the authors derived expressions for [Formula], which take into account all correlations between ionic movements in the electrolyte.  Thereby, [Formula] can be considerably smaller than [Formula], if strong directional correlations exist between the movements of cations and anions.  Finally, differences in Li+ transference nos. obtained by VLF-IS and by potentiostatic polarization measurements are discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoSzXPGV9JxYbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liwZMvfdOYmZQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XjtFKmtrk%253D&amp;md5=8be182b8bdb62df588b88c91119fc9f7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref69"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref69" class="rightTabRefNumLink">69</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit69"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kondou, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dokko, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Watanabe, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ueno, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Rate Performance of LiCoO2 Half-cells Using Highly Concentrated Lithium Bis(fluorosulfonyl)amide Electrolytes and Their Relevance to Transport Properties</span>. <i>Electrochemistry</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2021</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>21</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">00052</span></span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="casRecord empty" id="

                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i24">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c21557?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c21557</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Mayo–Lewis plot; <sup>1</sup>H NMR spectrum; GPC curve; flow curves; Raman spectra; ionic conductivity; self-diffusion coefficient; Nyquist plots; chronoamperograms; image of a Cu surface; XPS spectra; ATR-IR spectra; calculated IR spectra; and SEM image (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/suppl_file/am2c21557_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c21557/suppl_file/am2c21557_si_001.pdf">am2c21557_si_001.pdf (1.6 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

        <script nonce="90da89e9fdc9b325-MAN">
(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

</script>

<script nonce="90da89e9fdc9b325-MAN">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da89e9fdc9b325-MAN">

	/*

var infoCentralCapture = (function() {

    //Detect mobile device
    window.mobilecheck = function() {

u|g560|gene|gf\-5|g\-mo|go(\.w|od)|gr(ad|un)|haie|hcit|hd\-(m|p|t)|hei\-|hi(pt|ta)|hp( i|ip)|hs\-c|ht(c(\-| |_|a|g|p|s|t)|tp)|hu(aw|tc)|i\-(20|go|ma)|i230|iac( |\-|\/)|ibro|idea|ig01|ikom|im1k|inno|ipaq|iris|ja(t|v)a|jbro|jemu|jigs|kddi|keji|kgt( |\/)|klon|kpt |kwc\-|kyo(c|k)|le(no|xi)|lg( g|\/(k|l|u)|50|54|\-[a-w])|libw|lynx|m1\-w|m3ga|m50\/|ma(te|ui|xo)|mc(01|21|ca)|m\-cr|me(rc|ri)|mi(o8|oa|ts)|mmef|mo(01|02|bi|de|do|t(\-| |o|v)|zz)|mt(50|p1|v )|mwbp|mywa|n10[0-2]|n20[2-3]|n30(0|2)|n50(0|2|5)|n7(0(0|1)|10)|ne((c|m)\-|on|tf|wf|wg|wt)|nok(6|i)|nzph|o2im|op(ti|wv)|oran|owg1|p800|pan(a|d|t)|pdxg|pg(13|\-([1-8]|c))|phil|pire|pl(ay|uc)|pn\-2|po(ck|rt|se)|prox|psio|pt\-g|qa\-a|qc(07|12|21|32|60|\-[2-7]|i\-)|qtek|r380|r600|raks|rim9|ro(ve|zo)|s55\/|sa(ge|ma|mm|ms|ny|va)|sc(01|h\-|oo|p\-)|sdk\/|se(c(\-|0|1)|47|mc|nd|ri)|sgh\-|shar|sie(\-|m)|sk\-0|sl(45|id)|sm(al|ar|b3|it|t5)|so(ft|ny)|sp(01|h\-|v\-|v )|sy(01|mb)|t2(18|50)|t6(00|10|18)|ta(gt|lk)|tcl\-|tdg\-|tel(i|m)|tim\-|t\-mo|to(pl|sh)|ts(70|m\-|m3|m5)|tx\-9|up(\.b|g1|si)|utst|v400|v750|veri|vi(rg|te)|vk(40|5[0-3]|\-v)|vm40|voda|vulc|vx(52|53|60|61|70|80|81|83|85|98)|w3c(\-| )|webc|whit|wi(g |nc|nw)|wmlb|wonu|x700|yas\-|your|zeto|zte\-/i.test(a.substr(0,4))) check = true;})(navigator.userAgent||navigator.vendor||window.opera);
              return check;
        };

    var _isMobile = window.mobilecheck();
    if (_isMobile) {
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').addClass('mobile');
    }

    var emailValid = "",
        time = 3000,
        btnStatus = true,
        errorStatus = false,
        errormessage = "<p class='gdpr_err'><b>Please Enter a valid email address<b><p>";

    function init() {
        setTimeout(function() {
            var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
            if (cookieExist) {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
                return false;
            } else {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
                gdprMessage();
            }
            //Events
            $('#closebtn').on("click", hideForm);

            //Handle submit
            $('#gdpr-message-btn').on("click", function(event) {
                handleClick();
            });

            // //Hide if clicked outside div - TO DO stop handling click after submit
            // $(document).click(function(event) {
            // 	event.stopPropagation();
            //   console.log(event.target);
            //   if (!$(event.target).closest("#gdpr-message-content").length) {
            //     $("body").find("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
            //     $(this).off();
            //   }
            // });
            $('#gdpr-con-btn').click(function() {
                setCookie("gdpr", "true");
                hideForm();
            });
        }, time);
    }

    //Show the message
    function gdprMessage() {
        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $('#gdpr-message-modal').show();
    }

    function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
            //Submit the form
            //Hide form
            if ($('.gdpr_err')) {
                $('.gdpr_err').hide();
            }

            $("#gdpr-message-content")
                //If successful then...
            setCookie("gdpr", "true");
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");
        } else {
            //Show error message
            gdprErrorMessage();
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
    }

    function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
            return false;
        } else {
            errorStatus = true;
            $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
    }

    function validateEmail(email) {
        var re = /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
    }

    function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
    }

    function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + '=([^;]+)'));
        if (match) return match[1];
    }

    function hideForm() { //And set cookie
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag('event', 'GDPR Modal', { 'event_category': 'Pubs GDPR', 'event_label':'GDPR Dismissal'});
    }

    return {
        init: init
    }
})();

$(document).ready(infoCentralCapture.init);
</script>


                    
                        



        
        <script nonce="90da89e9fdc9b325-MAN">
var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>

    console.log("ACSPubs2 start");
    $.getJSON('https://extreme-ip-lookup.com/json/', function(data, status) {
            if ( status == 'success' ) {
                $.each(data, function(i, field){
                    cookie=cookie.concat(i+' = '+field+" - ");
                });
                
            } else {
                    cookie=status;
            }

            cookie1 = cookie;
            cookie = cookie1 + "; path=/; domain=.acs.org";
            //document.cookie = "ACSPubs2= "+ btoa(cookie) ;
            $.cookie("ACSPubs2", btoa(cookie), {path: '/'}); // Session cookie valid across the entire site.
            //TODO send cookie val to the controller for logging
            console.log("ACSPubs2 success");
    })
    .fail(function(xx, status, err){
        cookie="error: 127.0.0.1";
        //document.cookie = "ACSPubs2= "+ btoa(cookie) ;
        $.cookie("ACSPubs2", btoa(cookie), {path: '/'}); // Session cookie valid across the entire site.

        //TODO send cookie val to the controller for logging
    });
});
